## Chapter 15: Introduction to Traditional Design

### Introduction

The realm of architectural design is vast and varied, encompassing a multitude of styles, techniques, and philosophies. Among these, traditional design holds a unique place, representing the culmination of centuries of architectural evolution and cultural expression. This chapter, "Introduction to Traditional Design," aims to provide a comprehensive overview of the principles, processes, and applications of traditional design in the context of architectural construction and computation.

Traditional design is not a singular, monolithic concept, but rather a broad category encompassing a variety of styles that share common characteristics. These include an emphasis on harmony with the surrounding environment, the use of locally sourced materials, and adherence to time-tested construction techniques. In this chapter, we will delve into these principles, exploring how they inform the design process and shape the final architectural product.

The processes involved in traditional design are as diverse as the styles it encompasses. From the initial conceptualization to the final construction, each step is informed by a rich tapestry of historical precedent, cultural context, and practical considerations. We will examine these processes in detail, providing insight into the complex interplay of factors that contribute to the creation of a traditional design.

Finally, we will explore the applications of traditional design in contemporary architectural practice. Despite the advent of modern technologies and computational tools, traditional design continues to hold relevance, offering valuable lessons in sustainability, cultural sensitivity, and aesthetic appeal. We will discuss how these lessons can be integrated into modern design practices, enhancing the quality and relevance of contemporary architecture.

In summary, this chapter will provide a comprehensive introduction to the principles, processes, and applications of traditional design. Through a detailed exploration of this fascinating area of architectural design, we aim to provide readers with a deeper understanding of its significance and potential applications in the field of architectural construction and computation.

### Section: 15.1 Traditional Design Principles:

Traditional design principles are rooted in the history, culture, and environment of a particular region or community. They are often characterized by a deep respect for the past, a commitment to sustainability, and a focus on functionality and aesthetics. In this section, we will delve into the history of traditional design, explore its core principles, and examine how these principles are applied in contemporary architectural practice.

#### 15.1a History of Traditional Design

The history of traditional design is as diverse and complex as the cultures and societies that have shaped it. From the grandeur of classical Greek and Roman architecture to the simplicity and elegance of Japanese design, traditional design principles have evolved over centuries, reflecting the changing needs, values, and aspirations of societies around the world.

In the Western world, the period from 1700 to 1750 marked a significant shift in architectural design. The emphasis on symmetry, proportion, and classical motifs, which characterized the Renaissance period, gave way to a more ornate and decorative style, known as Rococo. This period also saw the emergence of the Neoclassical style, which sought to revive the principles of ancient Greek and Roman architecture.

Meanwhile, in the East, traditional design principles were being shaped by different cultural and philosophical influences. In Japan, for example, the concept of 'wabi-sabi' - the beauty of imperfection and transience - played a significant role in shaping traditional Japanese architecture. The use of natural materials, the emphasis on harmony with nature, and the minimalist aesthetic are all hallmarks of traditional Japanese design.

#### 15.1b Traditional Design Values

Traditional design values are deeply rooted in the history and culture of a particular region or community. They often reflect a deep respect for the past, a commitment to sustainability, and a focus on functionality and aesthetics. There are three main categories of traditional design values: tradition-based design value, the design value of restoration and preservation, and the vernacular design value.

##### Tradition-based Design Value

The tradition-based design value is based on the belief that traditional designs are the preferred typology and template for buildings and products. This is because they are considered to create timeless and functional designs. This value is often reflected in the use of classic architectural styles, such as Classicism, Vernacular, Restoration, and Preservation.

##### Design Value of Restoration and Preservation

The design value of restoration and preservation is based on a commitment to preserve the best of buildings and products for future generations. This value tends to represent restoring a building or product to its initial design and is usually rooted in three perspectives: historical, cultural, and environmental.

##### Vernacular Design Value

The vernacular design value is based on the use of local materials and techniques, and the adaptation of design to local climate and conditions. This value is often reflected in the use of traditional construction methods and materials, and the integration of buildings into their natural surroundings.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these traditional design values, exploring their origins, their implications for architectural design, and their relevance in the context of modern architectural practice.

#### 15.1b Principles and Elements

The principles and elements of traditional design are the fundamental building blocks that guide the creation of architectural structures. These principles are often derived from the natural world, cultural practices, and historical precedents. They are the rules and guidelines that architects use to create harmony, balance, and a sense of order in their designs.

##### 15.1b.1 Elements of Traditional Design

The elements of traditional design are the basic components that make up a structure. These include line, shape, form, space, color, and texture. Each of these elements plays a crucial role in defining the overall aesthetic and functional qualities of a building.

- **Line**: Lines can be horizontal, vertical, or diagonal. They define the edges and shape of a form, and can create a sense of direction or movement.
- **Shape**: Shapes are two-dimensional figures that are defined by their boundaries. They can be geometric (like squares and circles) or organic (like the shapes found in nature).
- **Form**: Forms are three-dimensional shapes. They have length, width, and depth, and can be viewed from multiple angles.
- **Space**: Space refers to the areas within, around, or between forms. It can be positive (filled with something) or negative (empty).
- **Color**: Color can create mood, define form, create depth, and establish a visual hierarchy.
- **Texture**: Texture refers to the surface quality of a form. It can be tactile (felt with the touch) or visual (perceived by the eye).

##### 15.1b.2 Principles of Traditional Design

The principles of traditional design are the rules that guide how the elements of design are organized and arranged. These include balance, proportion, rhythm, emphasis, and unity.

- **Balance**: Balance refers to the distribution of visual weight in a design. It can be symmetrical (equal weight on both sides of a central axis), asymmetrical (different but still balanced weight on either side of a central axis), or radial (elements arranged around a central point).
- **Proportion**: Proportion refers to the relative size and scale of the various elements in a design. It is often guided by ratios that are considered aesthetically pleasing, such as the Golden Ratio.
- **Rhythm**: Rhythm is created through the repetition of elements in a design. It can create a sense of movement and lead the viewer's eye through the design.
- **Emphasis**: Emphasis is created by making one element stand out from the others. It can be achieved through color, size, shape, or placement.
- **Unity**: Unity refers to the overall cohesion of a design. It is achieved when all the elements and principles of design work together to create a harmonious whole.

In the next section, we will explore how these principles and elements are applied in traditional architectural design, and how they continue to influence contemporary architectural practice.

#### 15.1c Traditional vs. Modern Design

The comparison between traditional and modern design is a topic of ongoing debate in the field of architecture. Both approaches have their unique merits and demerits, and the choice between the two often depends on the specific requirements of a project, the context, and the personal preferences of the architect and client.

##### 15.1c.1 Traditional Design

As discussed earlier, traditional design is deeply rooted in history, culture, and the natural world. It values the preservation of existing building traditions and aesthetics, and often seeks to create timeless and functional designs that fit seamlessly into their surroundings. Traditional design is often characterized by the use of local materials, craftsmanship, and techniques that have been passed down through generations.

The tradition-based design value, as mentioned in the related context, relies on the belief that traditional designs are the preferred typology and template for buildings and products. This approach often results in buildings that are harmonious with their environment and resonate with a sense of history and continuity.

##### 15.1c.2 Modern Design

On the other hand, modern design emerged in the 20th century as a response to rapid industrialization and technological advancements. It is characterized by a break from historical precedents and a focus on new ways of thinking and designing. Modern design values innovation, functionality, and simplicity, often resulting in designs that are minimalistic, efficient, and adaptable to changing needs.

Modern design often employs new materials and technologies, and it is not uncommon to see the use of steel, glass, and concrete in modern buildings. The designs are often geometric and asymmetrical, with a focus on open spaces and natural light.

##### 15.1c.3 Comparing Traditional and Modern Design

While traditional design values history, continuity, and craftsmanship, modern design values innovation, functionality, and simplicity. Traditional design often results in buildings that blend with their surroundings and evoke a sense of history, while modern design often results in buildings that stand out and reflect the spirit of the times.

However, it is important to note that these are not mutually exclusive approaches. Many architects successfully blend elements of both traditional and modern design to create buildings that are both innovative and respectful of their context. This approach, often referred to as "transitional design," seeks to bridge the gap between the old and the new, combining the best of both worlds to create designs that are both timeless and forward-looking.

### Section: 15.2 Design Process:

The design process in architecture is a complex and iterative procedure that involves a series of stages. These stages include conceptualization, schematic design, design development, construction documents, and construction administration. Each stage has its unique characteristics and requirements, and they collectively contribute to the creation of a successful architectural design.

#### 15.2a Conceptualization

Conceptualization is the initial stage of the design process where the architect begins to develop an abstract idea or concept for the project. This stage is primarily about understanding the project's requirements, context, and constraints, and then translating these into an initial design idea or concept.

The concept, as discussed in the related context, is an abstract object that represents an idea or a category. In the context of architectural design, a concept could represent a design idea, a design principle, a design strategy, or a design goal. For example, a concept could be about creating a building that blends with its natural surroundings, or it could be about designing a building that promotes social interactions among its users.

Concepts in architectural design are not limited to single words or "lexical concepts". They can also be complex ideas that are represented by phrases or sentences. For example, a concept could be "a building that uses natural ventilation to reduce energy consumption", or "a building that uses modular construction techniques to allow for future expansions".

Conceptualization in architectural design is not just about creating a design concept. It also involves testing and refining the concept to ensure that it is feasible and that it meets the project's requirements. This may involve creating sketches, models, or computer simulations to visualize the concept and to study its implications.

The conceptualization stage is crucial in the design process because it sets the direction for the subsequent stages. A well-conceived concept can lead to a successful design, while a poorly conceived concept can lead to problems later in the design process.

In the next section, we will discuss the next stage in the design process, which is the schematic design stage.

#### 15.2b Design Development

Following the conceptualization stage, the design development phase begins. This stage is where the initial concept is further refined and detailed. The design development phase is a critical stage in the architectural design process as it transforms the abstract ideas from the conceptualization stage into a more concrete and detailed design.

In the design development stage, the architect starts to consider more specific aspects of the design, such as the materials to be used, the structural system, the building services, and the construction techniques. The architect also starts to develop detailed drawings and models to represent the design. These drawings and models are not just about representing the physical appearance of the building, but they also represent the technical aspects of the design, such as the structural system, the building services, and the construction details.

The design development stage is also where the architect starts to consider the computational aspects of the design. With the advancement of technology, architects now have access to a wide range of computational tools that can assist them in the design process. These tools can be used to simulate the performance of the design, to optimize the design, and to generate complex geometries that would be difficult to achieve with traditional design methods.

For example, computational tools can be used to simulate the thermal performance of the building, to optimize the building's form for solar exposure, or to generate complex structural systems using parametric design techniques. These computational tools can greatly enhance the architect's ability to create innovative and high-performance designs.

However, the use of computational tools in the design process also requires a good understanding of the underlying principles and processes. The architect needs to understand how these tools work, what they can and cannot do, and how to interpret the results. This understanding is crucial for the effective use of computational tools in the design process.

The design development stage is not a linear process. It involves a lot of iteration and back-and-forth between the different aspects of the design. The architect may need to revise the design multiple times to address the various constraints and requirements. This iterative process is crucial for the creation of a successful architectural design.

In conclusion, the design development stage is where the initial concept is transformed into a detailed and concrete design. This stage involves a lot of iteration and refinement, and it requires a good understanding of the materials, the structural system, the building services, the construction techniques, and the computational tools. The design development stage is a critical stage in the architectural design process, and it plays a crucial role in the creation of a successful architectural design.

#### 15.2c Final Design

The final design phase is the culmination of all the previous stages of the architectural design process. It is where the architect finalizes the design and prepares the detailed construction documents that will be used to build the project. 

In the final design stage, the architect refines the design details and finalizes the selection of materials and construction techniques. The architect also finalizes the design of the building systems, such as the structural system, the mechanical system, and the electrical system. 

The final design stage also involves the preparation of detailed construction drawings and specifications. These documents provide the detailed information that the contractor will need to build the project. They include detailed drawings of the building's structure, the building's systems, and the building's finishes. They also include specifications that describe the quality and type of materials to be used, the construction techniques to be employed, and the standards to be met.

The use of computational tools continues to play a critical role in the final design stage. These tools can be used to refine the design, to optimize the performance of the building, and to generate the detailed construction documents. For example, computational tools can be used to perform detailed energy simulations, to optimize the building's energy performance, and to generate detailed construction drawings using Building Information Modeling (BIM) techniques.

However, the use of computational tools in the final design stage also requires a high level of expertise and understanding. The architect needs to understand how to use these tools effectively, how to interpret the results, and how to integrate the results into the final design. The architect also needs to understand the limitations of these tools and to be able to make informed decisions when these tools provide conflicting or ambiguous results.

In conclusion, the final design stage is a critical stage in the architectural design process. It is where the architect finalizes the design and prepares the detailed construction documents that will be used to build the project. The use of computational tools can greatly enhance the architect's ability to create high-quality and high-performance designs, but it also requires a high level of expertise and understanding.

#### 15.3a Hand Drafting

Hand drafting, also known as technical drawing, is a traditional design tool that has been used by architects and engineers for centuries. Despite the advent of computer-aided design (CAD) and other digital tools, hand drafting remains a fundamental skill in the field of architecture and construction.

Hand drafting involves the creation of detailed, scale drawings that represent the design of a building or structure. These drawings typically include floor plans, elevations, sections, and detailed views of specific elements of the design. They also often include annotations and notes that provide additional information about the design, such as the materials to be used, the construction techniques to be employed, and the standards to be met.

Hand drafting requires a high level of skill and precision. The drafter must be able to accurately represent the three-dimensional design in two dimensions, to scale the design accurately, and to depict the design in a clear and understandable way. The drafter must also have a thorough understanding of the principles of architectural design, the properties of the materials to be used, and the requirements of the construction process.

Despite the rise of digital tools, hand drafting continues to be a valuable skill in the field of architecture and construction. Hand drafting can provide a more intuitive and tactile understanding of the design, which can be particularly valuable in the early stages of the design process. Hand drafting can also be a useful tool for communicating ideas and concepts to clients, colleagues, and other stakeholders.

However, hand drafting also has its limitations. It can be time-consuming and labor-intensive, particularly for complex designs. It can also be difficult to make changes or revisions to a hand-drawn design. For these reasons, hand drafting is often used in conjunction with digital tools, such as CAD software, which can provide a more efficient and flexible way of creating, modifying, and sharing design drawings.

In conclusion, while hand drafting may no longer be the primary tool for architectural design, it remains a valuable skill that can complement and enhance the use of digital tools. As such, it is important for students of architecture and construction to develop a strong foundation in hand drafting techniques, as well as an understanding of how these techniques can be integrated with digital tools in the design process.

#### 15.3b Computer-Aided Design

Computer-Aided Design (CAD) represents a significant leap forward in the field of architectural design and construction. As a tool, it has revolutionized the way architects, engineers, and draftsmen work, merging their roles and empowering them with the ability to perform complex tasks with greater efficiency and precision.

CAD systems began to gain traction in the mid-1960s, starting with the IBM Drafting System. These systems offered more than just electronic drafting; they provided capabilities that are now often taken for granted in modern computer systems. These include automated generation of bills of materials, auto layout in integrated circuits, interference checking, and many others. Over time, CAD systems evolved to perform engineering calculations, a task that was previously done by hand or by individuals who could run computer programs.

Modern CAD software packages range from 2D vector-based drafting systems to 3D solid and surface modelers. They allow for rotations in three dimensions, providing the ability to view a designed object from any desired angle, even from the inside looking out. Some CAD software is capable of dynamic mathematical modeling, further enhancing the capabilities of the designer.

CAD technology is used extensively in the design of tools, machinery, and all types of buildings, from small residential houses to large commercial and industrial structures such as hospitals and factories. It is primarily used for detailed engineering of 3D models or 2D drawings of physical components. However, its use extends throughout the engineering process, from conceptual design and layout of products, through strength and dynamic analysis of assemblies, to the definition of manufacturing methods.

The benefits of CAD over traditional hand drafting are numerous. It offers a more efficient and flexible way of designing, allowing for easy modifications and revisions. It also enables the designer to perform complex calculations and simulations, which can help in optimizing the design and predicting its performance under various conditions. Furthermore, CAD systems can generate detailed and accurate bills of materials, which can significantly simplify the procurement and construction processes.

However, like any tool, CAD also has its limitations. It requires a significant investment in hardware and software, as well as in training. It can also be less intuitive than hand drafting, particularly for those who are not familiar with the software. Despite these challenges, the benefits of CAD generally outweigh its drawbacks, making it an indispensable tool in the field of architectural design and construction.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the specific features and capabilities of CAD software, and how they can be effectively utilized in the design and construction process.

#### 15.3c 3D Modeling

3D modeling is a critical tool in the architectural design process, allowing architects to visualize and manipulate their designs in a virtual environment. This technology has evolved significantly over the years, with numerous software solutions available that cater to different needs and preferences.

One such solution is Solid Modeling Solutions (SMS), which offers a suite of libraries for various aspects of 3D modeling. These include SMLib, a fully functional non-manifold topological structure and solid modeling functionality; TSNLib, which analyzes NURBS based trimmed surface representations; GSNLib, based on NLib with curve/curve and surface/surface intersection capabilities; NLib, an advanced geometric modeling kernel based on NURBS curves and surfaces; VSLib, which offers deformable modeling using the constrained optimization techniques of the calculus of variations; and PolyMLib, an object-oriented software toolkit library that provides a set of objects and corresponding methods to repair, optimize, review, and edit triangle mesh models.

Another popular 3D modeling software is Wings 3D, a free and open-source subdivision modeler inspired by Nendo and Mirai from Izware. Named after the winged-edge data structure it uses internally to store coordinate and adjacency data, Wings 3D is available for Windows, Linux, and Mac OS X, using the Erlang environment. It can be used to model and texture low to mid-range polygon models. While it does not support animations and has only basic OpenGL rendering facilities, it can export to external rendering software such as POV-Ray and YafRay.

3D modeling software like SMS and Wings 3D offer architects a powerful tool to visualize, manipulate, and optimize their designs. They allow for rotations in three dimensions, providing the ability to view a designed object from any desired angle, even from the inside looking out. This capability is particularly useful in architectural design, where understanding the spatial relationships and interactions between different elements of a design is crucial.

In addition to visualization, 3D modeling software also offers a range of computational tools that can aid in the design process. These include capabilities for analyzing the structural integrity of a design, optimizing its performance, and even simulating its behavior under various conditions. These computational tools can provide valuable insights that can inform and improve the design process.

In conclusion, 3D modeling is an essential tool in modern architectural design. It offers a powerful means of visualizing and manipulating designs, as well as a suite of computational tools that can aid in the design process. Whether you're designing a small residential house or a large commercial structure, 3D modeling software can provide the tools you need to bring your vision to life.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the principles, processes, and applications of traditional design in architectural construction. We have delved into the importance of understanding the historical and cultural contexts of traditional design, as well as the technical aspects that make it a unique and valuable approach in the field of architecture. 

We have also discussed the role of computation in traditional design, highlighting how modern technology can be used to enhance and streamline traditional design processes. From digital modeling to computational analysis, we have seen how these tools can help architects to create designs that are both aesthetically pleasing and structurally sound.

In conclusion, traditional design in architectural construction is a complex and multifaceted discipline that requires a deep understanding of both historical context and modern technology. By combining these two elements, architects can create designs that honor the past while also embracing the future.

## Chapter 16: Field Trip

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will embark on an exciting journey, a field trip, to explore the practical applications of the principles, processes, and computations we have discussed so far in architectural construction. This chapter will provide a unique opportunity to observe and understand how the theoretical concepts of architectural construction and computation are applied in real-world scenarios.

The field trip will take us through various architectural sites, where we will witness the implementation of computational design and construction techniques. We will observe how architects and engineers leverage advanced computational tools to design, analyze, and construct architectural structures. This will provide a deeper understanding of the intricate relationship between architectural design, construction, and computation.

We will also delve into the challenges faced in the field, and how professionals overcome these obstacles using computational methods. This will not only enhance our understanding of the subject matter but also provide valuable insights into the practical aspects of architectural construction and computation.

This chapter will not only be informative but also interactive, encouraging readers to engage with the material and apply their knowledge in a practical context. So, let's gear up for this exciting field trip and delve deeper into the world of architectural construction and computation.

### Section: 16.1 Visiting Construction Sites

#### Subsection: 16.1a Site Selection

The first step in our field trip is the selection of construction sites to visit. The sites chosen for this trip are not random, but carefully selected to provide a comprehensive understanding of the application of computational methods in architectural construction. The sites vary in their geographical location, type of construction, and the specific computational methods employed.

The sites we will be visiting include:

1. **Minesing Wetlands**: This site is a Ramsar site in Canada, known for its unique wetland construction. The architects and engineers here have used computational methods to design structures that are sustainable and blend seamlessly with the natural environment. The use of computational fluid dynamics (CFD) to model water flow and the application of geographic information system (GIS) for site analysis are some of the computational methods employed here.

2. **Elizabeth Bay House**: This site in Australia has undergone several modifications over the years. The use of computational methods in the renovation and restoration of this historic building provides an interesting case study. The use of 3D scanning and modeling techniques to document and analyze the existing structure is a key computational method used here.

3. **Escarpment Mine Project**: This site in New Zealand is a large-scale mining project. The use of computational methods in the design and construction of mining infrastructure is a key focus here. The application of geotechnical engineering software for slope stability analysis and the use of finite element method (FEM) for structural analysis are some of the computational methods employed here.

4. **'s Lands Zeemagazijn**: This site in the Netherlands is a historic maritime warehouse that has been converted into a museum. The use of computational methods in the adaptive reuse of historic buildings is a key focus here. The use of building information modeling (BIM) for the documentation and management of the renovation process is a key computational method used here.

5. **Victoria Mine**: This site in the United States is a historic mining site. The use of computational methods in the preservation and interpretation of historic industrial sites is a key focus here. The use of 3D laser scanning and photogrammetry for the documentation of the site and the use of virtual reality (VR) for the interpretation of the site are some of the computational methods employed here.

6. **Port Radium**: This site in Canada is a former uranium mine. The use of computational methods in the remediation and reclamation of contaminated sites is a key focus here. The use of GIS for site analysis and the application of environmental modeling software for the prediction of contaminant transport are some of the computational methods employed here.

7. **Brett Whiteley House**: This site in Australia is a historic residence. The use of computational methods in the preservation and interpretation of historic residences is a key focus here. The use of 3D laser scanning and photogrammetry for the documentation of the site and the use of augmented reality (AR) for the interpretation of the site are some of the computational methods employed here.

8. **El Per (Maya site)**: This site in Guatemala is a historic Maya archaeological site. The use of computational methods in the documentation and interpretation of archaeological sites is a key focus here. The use of remote sensing techniques for site discovery and the application of 3D modeling techniques for the interpretation of the site are some of the computational methods employed here.

These sites provide a diverse range of applications of computational methods in architectural construction. By visiting these sites, we will gain a deeper understanding of how these methods are applied in practice.

#### Subsection: 16.1b Site Analysis

After the selection of construction sites, the next step in our field trip is the analysis of these sites. Site analysis is a crucial part of architectural construction and computation as it provides the necessary data to inform the design and construction process. It involves the study of the physical and environmental conditions of the site, as well as the application of computational methods to analyze and interpret this data.

Let's delve into the site analysis of the selected sites:

1. **Minesing Wetlands**: The site analysis for Minesing Wetlands involves the study of the unique wetland environment. This includes the analysis of soil conditions, water flow, and the local ecosystem. Computational methods such as GIS are used to map and analyze the site's topography, hydrology, and vegetation. CFD is used to model water flow and inform the design of structures that can withstand the dynamic wetland environment.

2. **Elizabeth Bay House**: The site analysis for Elizabeth Bay House involves the study of the historic building and its surroundings. This includes the analysis of the building's structure, materials, and modifications over the years. Computational methods such as 3D scanning and modeling are used to document and analyze the existing structure. This data is then used to inform the renovation and restoration process, ensuring that the historic integrity of the building is preserved.

3. **Escarpment Mine Project**: The site analysis for the Escarpment Mine Project involves the study of the geotechnical conditions of the site. This includes the analysis of soil and rock properties, slope stability, and seismic activity. Computational methods such as geotechnical engineering software and FEM are used to analyze these conditions and inform the design and construction of mining infrastructure.

4. **'s Lands Zeemagazijn**: The site analysis for 's Lands Zeemagazijn involves the study of the historic maritime warehouse and its surroundings. This includes the analysis of the building's structure, materials, and modifications over the years. Computational methods such as building information modeling (BIM) are used to document and analyze the existing structure. This data is then used to inform the adaptive reuse process, ensuring that the historic integrity of the building is preserved while making it suitable for modern use.

In each of these cases, site analysis provides the necessary data to inform the design and construction process. The application of computational methods allows for a more accurate and efficient analysis, leading to better-informed decisions and more successful outcomes.

#### Subsection: 16.1c Site Documentation

After the site analysis, the next step in our field trip is the documentation of the construction sites. Site documentation is an essential part of architectural construction and computation as it records the existing conditions of the site and the findings from the site analysis. This documentation serves as a reference throughout the design and construction process and can be used to validate computational models and simulations.

Let's look at the site documentation process for the selected sites:

1. **Minesing Wetlands**: The site documentation for Minesing Wetlands includes the collection of soil samples, water samples, and ecological data. This data is recorded and organized in a GIS database, along with the topographic, hydrologic, and vegetation maps created during the site analysis. Photographs and drone footage are also taken to visually document the site. 

2. **Elizabeth Bay House**: The site documentation for Elizabeth Bay House includes the creation of a detailed 3D model of the existing structure, based on the data collected during the 3D scanning process. This model is accompanied by a report that documents the building's structure, materials, and modifications over the years. Historical records and photographs are also collected to provide additional context.

3. **Escarpment Mine Project**: The site documentation for the Escarpment Mine Project includes the collection of soil and rock samples, as well as data on slope stability and seismic activity. This data is recorded and organized in a geotechnical database, along with the results of the FEM simulations conducted during the site analysis. Photographs and drone footage are also taken to visually document the site.

4. **'s Lands Zeemagazijn**: The site documentation for 's Lands Zeemagazijn includes the creation of a detailed 3D model of the existing structure, based on the data collected during the 3D scanning process. This model is accompanied by a report that documents the building's structure, materials, and modifications over the years. Historical records and photographs are also collected to provide additional context.

In all cases, the site documentation is stored in a digital format that allows for easy access and sharing among the project team. This digital documentation can also be integrated with computational tools for further analysis and design development. 

In the next section, we will discuss how this site documentation is used in the design process.

#### Subsection: 16.2a Construction Methods

In this section, we will observe and analyze various construction methods used in the field. Understanding these methods is crucial for architects and civil engineers as they provide the foundation for the creation of physical structures such as buildings, bridges, or railways.

1. **Brick Construction**: One of the oldest and most common construction methods is brick construction. Bricks are small rectangular blocks typically used to form parts of buildings, especially walls. Before 7,000 BC, bricks were hand-molded from mud and dried by the sun. However, during the Industrial Revolution, mass-produced bricks became a common alternative to stone due to their lower cost, predictability, and ease of handling. Bricks are small and easy to handle, strong in compression, durable, and require low maintenance. They can be formed into complex shapes, providing ample opportunities for architectural design.

2. **Stone Construction**: Stone construction is another traditional method that has been used for thousands of years. Stone is a natural material that is widely available and provides a unique aesthetic appeal. However, it is typically more expensive than brick, less predictable, and more difficult to handle. Despite these challenges, stone construction is still used today for specific applications where its aesthetic and structural properties are desired.

3. **Concrete Construction**: Concrete is a versatile and widely used construction material. It is strong in compression, relatively inexpensive, and can be cast into a variety of shapes. However, it is weak in tension, which is often addressed by using reinforced concrete, where steel bars are embedded within the concrete to provide tensile strength.

4. **Steel Construction**: Steel is a common material used in modern construction, especially for large structures like bridges and skyscrapers. It is strong in both tension and compression, and its properties can be precisely controlled during manufacturing. However, it is susceptible to corrosion and requires protective coatings or treatments.

5. **Wood Construction**: Wood is a renewable material that is widely used in residential construction. It is relatively easy to work with and provides good insulation properties. However, it is susceptible to decay and fire, and its properties can vary significantly depending on the species and treatment of the wood.

6. **Composite Construction**: Composite construction involves the use of two or more different materials that are combined to exploit the strengths of each. A common example is reinforced concrete, where concrete provides strength in compression and steel provides strength in tension.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these construction methods, exploring their principles, processes, and applications in architectural construction and computation.

#### Subsection: 16.2b Material Selection

In the process of architectural construction, the selection of materials is a critical step that can significantly influence the final outcome of the project. The choice of materials not only affects the structural integrity and longevity of the construction but also its aesthetic appeal and environmental impact. 

1. **Metallic Materials**: Metals such as steel, aluminum, and copper are commonly used in construction due to their strength, durability, and resistance to environmental conditions. For instance, steel is often used in the construction of large structures like bridges and skyscrapers due to its strength in both tension and compression. Austenitic stainless steel, a specific type of steel, is known for its superior corrosion resistance and is often used in environments where corrosion is a concern. 

2. **Ceramic Materials**: Ceramic materials like bricks and concrete are widely used in construction. Bricks, made from clay, are strong in compression, durable, and require low maintenance. Concrete, on the other hand, is a versatile material that is strong in compression and can be cast into a variety of shapes. However, it is weak in tension, which is often addressed by using reinforced concrete, where steel bars are embedded within the concrete to provide tensile strength.

3. **Composite Materials**: Composite materials are made from two or more constituent materials with significantly different physical or chemical properties. These materials are designed to have characteristics superior to those of the individual components. An example of a composite material used in construction is fiberglass, which is lightweight, strong, and resistant to environmental conditions.

4. **Polymeric Materials**: Polymeric materials, such as plastics and rubbers, are increasingly being used in construction due to their versatility, durability, and resistance to environmental conditions. For instance, PVC (polyvinyl chloride) is commonly used in the construction of pipes and fittings due to its resistance to corrosion and chemical attack.

The selection of the best material overall involves considering various performance indices. For tension, the performance index is given by $P_{CR}=\sigma/\rho$, where $\sigma$ is the stress and $\rho$ is the density of the material. For bending, the performance index is given by $P_{CR}=\sqrt{\sigma}/\rho$. These indices can be plotted on a log-log graph to compare the performance of different materials. 

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the computational aspects of architectural construction, exploring how computational tools can aid in the design and construction process.

#### Subsection: 16.2c Construction Management

Construction management is a critical aspect of architectural construction that involves planning, coordinating, and controlling a project from inception to completion. It is aimed at meeting the client's requirements to produce a functionally and financially viable project. 

1. **Planning**: This is the initial stage of construction management. It involves defining the project's objectives, including scope, scheduling, budgeting, setting performance requirements, and selecting project participants. The planning stage also includes the use of methodologies underpinning Building Information Modeling (BIM) to create a virtual design of the construction. This allows for better visualization, improved collaboration, and greater speed and efficiency in construction.

2. **Coordinating**: This stage involves the organization and synchronization of the various aspects of the project. It includes coordinating the work of the design team and the construction team, ensuring that all necessary materials are available when needed, and that all necessary permits and licenses have been obtained. The spacetime dimensions concept in VDC plays a crucial role in this stage, as it allows for the coordination of activities in both space and time, ensuring that the project progresses smoothly and efficiently.

3. **Controlling**: This stage involves monitoring the progress of the project to ensure that it is on track with the planned schedule and budget. It includes quality control, cost control, and time management. The use of applied ontology based in the upper ontology, as mentioned in the related context, can be beneficial in this stage. It can help in capturing the present status of construction activities and identifying any deviations from the plan.

4. **Completion**: This is the final stage of construction management. It involves ensuring that the project has been completed according to the set objectives and that all contractual obligations have been met. It also includes a post-construction review to identify any lessons learned that can be applied to future projects.

In conclusion, construction management is a complex process that requires a deep understanding of construction techniques, materials, and computational tools. It is a critical aspect of architectural construction that can significantly influence the success of a project.

### Section: 16.3 Documenting Field Trip Findings:

#### Subsection: 16.3a Note Taking

Note-taking is an essential skill for documenting field trip findings. It allows you to capture important details, observations, and insights that you can later refer to when analyzing and interpreting your data. There are several methods of note-taking, and the choice of method often depends on the nature of the information being documented and personal preference. 

##### Linear Note-taking

Linear note-taking is the process of recording information in the order in which you receive it. This method is typically used to create chronological outlines of observations or experiences. However, it's important to be mindful not to transcribe everything verbatim, but rather to focus on capturing the key points and details. 

##### Outlining

Outlining is another common note-taking system. It involves organizing notes and thoughts in a structured, logical manner, which can save time during the review and analysis stages. Outlines typically use headings and bullets to structure information, with different levels of information represented by Roman numerals, letters of the alphabet, and Arabic numerals. 

However, this method can be limiting in non-digital form, as it can be difficult to insert additional information later. To overcome this, you can use adaptive systems such as leaving large spaces between items or using the reverse side of the preceding page in a spiral notebook for insertions. 

##### Sentence Method

The sentence method involves writing down each topic or observation as a short, simple sentence. This method can be particularly useful for documenting field trip findings, as it allows for concise, clear documentation of observations. 

##### Computerized Note-taking

With the advancement of technology, computerized note-taking has become increasingly popular. This method, whether with a word processor, outliner software, or a digital notebook program, allows for easy revisions and additions to the outline. It can be particularly useful for field trips where a large amount of data is being collected, as it allows for efficient organization and retrieval of information.

In conclusion, the method of note-taking you choose for documenting field trip findings should align with your personal preference and the nature of the data being collected. Regardless of the method chosen, the key is to ensure that the notes are clear, concise, and accurately capture the important details and observations.

#### Subsection: 16.3b Photography

Photography is another crucial tool for documenting field trip findings in architectural construction and computation. It provides a visual record of the site, structures, and processes observed during the field trip. This section will discuss the different types of photography and how they can be used in documenting field trip findings.

##### Digital Photography

Digital photography has become the standard in many fields due to its convenience and versatility. Cameras such as the Canon Digital IXUS and the Pixel 3a can capture high-quality images that can be easily transferred to a computer for further analysis. These images retain original Exif information, which includes details about the camera settings used to take the photo, providing valuable context for the image.

##### Film Photography

Despite the prevalence of digital photography, film photography still has its place in field documentation. Different types of photographic film, such as Ilford Photo's graded, variable contrast, digital panchromatic, and direct positive films, offer unique characteristics that can enhance the visual documentation of a field trip. However, film photography requires more resources and time for developing and scanning the images.

##### Strip Photography

Strip photography is a technique that captures a series of images in a strip format. This technique can be useful for documenting long, continuous structures or processes, such as the N700 series Shinkansen. However, it requires specialized equipment and expertise to execute effectively.

##### Satellite Imagery

Satellite imagery, such as those captured by Sentinel-2, can provide a broader perspective of the field trip site. This can be particularly useful for documenting large-scale structures or landscapes. However, access to up-to-date satellite imagery may be limited or costly.

##### Lens Selection

The choice of lens can significantly affect the quality and type of images you can capture. For instance, the Canon TS-E 90mm lens allows for tilt-shift photography, which can be useful for architectural photography. On the other hand, the Canon EF 200mm lens is a telephoto lens that can capture distant subjects in detail.

In conclusion, photography is a versatile tool for documenting field trip findings. The choice of camera, film or digital, the technique used, and the lens selection can all impact the quality and type of documentation you can achieve. Therefore, it's important to plan your photographic documentation strategy carefully before embarking on a field trip.

#### Subsection: 16.3c Report Writing

After the field trip and the collection of data through various methods such as photography, the next step is to compile and present these findings in a comprehensive report. This section will discuss the process of report writing, including the structure of the report, the inclusion of visual data, and the importance of clear and concise writing.

##### Report Structure

A well-structured report is crucial for effectively communicating the findings of a field trip. The report should begin with an introduction that provides an overview of the field trip, including the purpose, location, and date of the trip. This should be followed by a detailed description of the observations made during the trip, organized in a logical manner. For instance, the observations could be presented in the order they were made, or they could be grouped by theme or topic.

The report should also include an analysis section, where the observations are interpreted and their implications discussed. This section should draw on the principles and concepts discussed in earlier chapters of this book, such as the principles of architectural construction and computation. Finally, the report should conclude with a summary of the findings and any recommendations for further study or action.

##### Inclusion of Visual Data

Visual data, such as photographs and diagrams, can greatly enhance the clarity and impact of a field trip report. These should be integrated into the report in a way that supports the text, rather than simply being appended at the end. For example, a photograph of a building could be included alongside a description of its architectural features, or a diagram illustrating a construction process could be included in the analysis section to help explain the process.

When including visual data, it is important to provide clear captions and references. The caption should describe what the image or diagram shows and how it relates to the text. The reference should provide information about the source of the image or diagram, if it is not original.

##### Clear and Concise Writing

Clear and concise writing is essential for an effective field trip report. The report should be written in a formal, academic style, with complete sentences and proper grammar. Technical terms should be used accurately and consistently, and any jargon should be defined when it is first used.

The report should also be concise, with each sentence contributing to the overall purpose of the report. Unnecessary details and digressions should be avoided. However, it is also important to provide enough detail to allow the reader to understand the observations and analyses.

In conclusion, report writing is a critical part of documenting field trip findings in architectural construction and computation. A well-written report can effectively communicate the findings of a field trip, contributing to the body of knowledge in the field and informing future research and practice.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we embarked on a virtual field trip, exploring the intersection of architectural construction and computation. We delved into the principles, processes, and applications of computational design in architecture, and how it is revolutionizing the field. We saw how computational tools and techniques are being used to design and construct buildings that are not only aesthetically pleasing but also structurally sound and environmentally friendly. 

We also examined the role of computation in the architectural design process, from the initial concept to the final construction. We saw how architects are using computational design to create complex geometries, optimize structural performance, and reduce environmental impact. We also explored how computational tools are being used to simulate and analyze building performance, helping architects to make informed design decisions.

Finally, we looked at some of the challenges and opportunities in the field of architectural computation. While the use of computational tools in architecture presents some challenges, such as the need for specialized skills and the potential for over-reliance on technology, it also offers exciting opportunities for innovation and creativity. As we move forward, it will be interesting to see how the field of architectural computation continues to evolve and shape the future of architecture.
