## Chapter: Chapter 19: Presentation on Design

### Introduction

The art of architectural design is a complex and intricate process that requires a deep understanding of both the principles of architecture and the tools of computation. In this chapter, we will delve into the world of architectural presentations, exploring how computational tools can be utilized to effectively communicate design ideas and concepts.

The presentation of architectural designs is a critical aspect of the architectural process. It is through these presentations that architects communicate their vision, not only to clients but also to other stakeholders such as contractors, engineers, and city planners. The ability to effectively present a design can make the difference between a project's success or failure.

In the era of digital technology, the tools and techniques used in architectural presentations have evolved significantly. The use of computational tools has become increasingly prevalent, allowing architects to create more detailed and realistic representations of their designs. These tools not only enhance the visual appeal of the presentations but also improve the accuracy and efficiency of the design process.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles and processes involved in creating effective architectural presentations using computational tools. We will discuss the various types of software and technologies available, and how they can be used to create different types of presentations. We will also look at the role of computation in the design process, and how it can be used to solve complex architectural problems.

Whether you are an experienced architect looking to enhance your presentation skills, or a student just starting out in the field, this chapter will provide you with a comprehensive understanding of the role of computation in architectural presentations. So, let's embark on this journey together, exploring the fascinating intersection of architecture, computation, and design presentation.

### Section: 19.1 Design Presentations:

#### 19.1a Presentation Preparation

The preparation of an architectural design presentation is a meticulous process that requires careful planning and execution. The first step in this process is understanding the audience. The presentation should be tailored to the needs and expectations of the audience, whether they are clients, contractors, engineers, or city planners. The level of technical detail, the use of jargon, and the emphasis on certain aspects of the design can all vary depending on the audience.

The second step is defining the objectives of the presentation. These objectives can range from persuading a client to approve a design, to explaining the technical details of a project to a team of engineers. The objectives will guide the content and structure of the presentation.

The third step is selecting the appropriate computational tools. There is a wide range of software and technologies available for architectural presentations, each with its own strengths and weaknesses. Some tools are better suited for creating realistic visualizations, while others are more effective for technical drawings or interactive presentations. The choice of tools should be guided by the objectives of the presentation and the needs of the audience.

Once the audience, objectives, and tools have been defined, the next step is to create the content of the presentation. This involves translating the architectural design into a format that can be easily understood and appreciated by the audience. This can involve creating 3D models, technical drawings, animations, or interactive simulations. The use of computational tools can greatly enhance this process, allowing for a higher level of detail and realism than would be possible with traditional methods.

The final step in the preparation process is rehearsing the presentation. This is a crucial step that is often overlooked. Rehearsing allows the presenter to refine their delivery, identify any potential issues, and become more comfortable with the material. It also provides an opportunity to test the technical aspects of the presentation, such as the operation of the software and the display of the visualizations.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into each of these steps, providing practical advice and examples to help you prepare effective architectural presentations.

#### 19.1b Presentation Techniques

The techniques used in architectural design presentations can greatly influence the effectiveness of the communication. These techniques can be broadly categorized into visual, verbal, and interactive techniques.

##### Visual Techniques

Visual techniques involve the use of images, diagrams, models, and animations to convey the design. The choice of visual techniques depends on the nature of the design and the objectives of the presentation. For instance, 3D models and animations can be used to provide a realistic visualization of the design, while diagrams and technical drawings can be used to explain the structural and functional aspects of the design.

Computational tools play a crucial role in the creation of visual content. Software such as Autodesk's suite (including Smoke, Flame, and Maya), Adobe Systems' After Effects and Photoshop, and others like Final Cut Pro and Avid, offer a wide range of capabilities for creating high-quality visual content. These tools allow for the creation of detailed and realistic 3D models, sophisticated animations, and high-quality images and videos.

##### Verbal Techniques

Verbal techniques involve the use of language to explain and discuss the design. This can include the use of technical jargon, metaphors, and storytelling. The choice of language should be tailored to the audience. For instance, technical jargon may be appropriate when presenting to a team of engineers, but may be confusing or off-putting to a client or a non-technical audience.

The use of storytelling can be particularly effective in architectural presentations. By framing the design as a story, the presenter can engage the audience's emotions and imagination, making the design more memorable and compelling.

##### Interactive Techniques

Interactive techniques involve the use of interactive media and technologies to engage the audience in the presentation. This can include the use of interactive 3D models, virtual reality, augmented reality, and interactive simulations. These techniques can provide a more immersive and engaging experience for the audience, allowing them to explore the design in a more intuitive and natural way.

The choice of interactive techniques should be guided by the objectives of the presentation and the needs of the audience. For instance, virtual reality might be an effective tool for presenting a design to a client, allowing them to experience the design in a realistic and immersive way. However, it might not be as effective for presenting technical details to a team of engineers.

In conclusion, the choice of presentation techniques should be guided by the objectives of the presentation, the needs of the audience, and the nature of the design. By carefully selecting and combining visual, verbal, and interactive techniques, the presenter can create a compelling and effective presentation.

#### 19.1c Audience Engagement

Engaging the audience is a crucial aspect of architectural design presentations. It involves capturing and maintaining the audience's attention, stimulating their interest, and encouraging their participation. This can be achieved through a combination of effective presentation techniques and the use of audience response systems.

##### Audience Response Systems

Audience response systems are interactive technologies that enable presenters to solicit and collect responses from the audience in real time. These systems can be used to gauge the audience's understanding, gather their opinions, and stimulate discussion. They can also provide a valuable source of feedback, allowing presenters to adjust their presentations based on the audience's responses.

Web-based audience response systems are particularly versatile, as they can work with a variety of computing devices, including notebook computers, smartphones, and PDAs. These devices can connect to the Internet via Wi-Fi, allowing participants to log into a server and input their responses to questions presented on a set of web pages. The summarized responses can then be displayed through a projector and on each participant's device[^1^].

The Internet has also made it possible to gather audience responses on a massive scale. For instance, during the 2013 State of The Union address by President Barack Obama, Microsoft featured Bing Pulse, a system that allowed registered users to input their responses to the address and visualized the results as a trending graph in real time[^1^].

##### Engaging the Audience

To engage the audience effectively, presenters should consider the following strategies:

- **Interactivity**: Use interactive media and technologies to involve the audience in the presentation. This can include the use of interactive 3D models, virtual reality, and audience response systems.

- **Storytelling**: Frame the design as a story to engage the audience's emotions and imagination. This can make the design more memorable and compelling.

- **Language**: Tailor the language to the audience. Avoid technical jargon when presenting to a non-technical audience, and use metaphors and analogies to explain complex concepts.

- **Visuals**: Use high-quality visuals to convey the design. This can include images, diagrams, models, and animations. Computational tools such as Autodesk's suite, Adobe Systems' After Effects and Photoshop, and others like Final Cut Pro and Avid, can be used to create high-quality visual content[^2^].

By employing these strategies, presenters can create engaging and effective architectural design presentations.

[^1^]: Web-based audience response systems and their applications, Arrow of Time (Numbers), Reception, Internet.
[^2^]: Presentation Techniques, Architectural Construction and Computation: Principles, Processes, and Applications, Chapter 19: Presentation on Design, Section: 19.1b Presentation Techniques.

### Section: 19.2 Design Documentation:

#### 19.2a Drawing Standards

In architectural design, drawing standards are crucial for maintaining consistency, clarity, and communication across different stages of the design process. These standards encompass various aspects of design documentation, including CAD standards, line thickness, text and dimension, and file naming standards.

##### CAD Standards

Computer-Aided Design (CAD) standards are a set of guidelines that govern the use of CAD tools in architectural design. These standards ensure that all design documents are consistent, easy to interpret, and compatible with different software and hardware configurations. The most common CAD standards in the Architecture, Engineering, and Construction (AEC) industry include layer standards, which define the organization and naming of layers in a CAD drawing[^1^].

For instance, a standardized layer might be named "A-Wall" for architectural walls or "E-Light" for electrical lighting fixtures. These standardized layers help in organizing the drawing and make it easier for other professionals to understand and work with the design[^1^].

##### Line Thickness

Line thickness is another important aspect of drawing standards. It helps in distinguishing between different elements in a design and provides a visual hierarchy. For example, in AutoCAD, parts to be printed in black are usually drawn in 1 to 7 basic colors, with each color representing a different line thickness. The line thickness for pens and plot can range from 0.13 mm (Gray) to 1.00 mm (Green)[^1^].

##### Text and Dimension

Text and dimension standards govern the use of text and dimensions in design documents. These standards specify the heights and font styles to be used for text and dimensions. For instance, the heights can range from 2.5 mm to 7.0 mm, with the stroke thickness (lineweight) being 0.1 of the character height[^1^].

The font styles commonly used include "Romans.shx  Romantic Simplex" and "ISOCPEUR.ttf". In some cases, screen fonts like Arial and Times New Roman may also be used[^1^].

##### File Naming Standards

File naming standards are crucial for organizing and managing design documents. These standards specify the format and structure of file names, making it easier to identify and locate specific files. For example, a file name might include the project number, discipline, zone, view, level, content, and sequential number[^1^].

In conclusion, drawing standards play a vital role in architectural design. They ensure consistency, clarity, and communication across different stages of the design process, making it easier for architects, engineers, and construction professionals to collaborate and bring a design to life.

[^1^]: Eastman, C., Teicholz, P., Sacks, R., & Liston, K. (2011). BIM Handbook: A Guide to Building Information Modeling for Owners, Managers, Designers, Engineers and Contractors. John Wiley & Sons.

#### 19.2b Documentation Techniques

In addition to the drawing standards, there are several documentation techniques that architects use to communicate their design ideas effectively. These techniques include the use of different types of drawings, models, and computational tools.

##### Types of Drawings

Architectural design documentation typically includes several types of drawings, each serving a specific purpose[^2^]:

- **Conceptual Drawings**: These are initial sketches that capture the basic ideas of the design. They are often hand-drawn and may include rough floor plans, elevations, and perspective views.

- **Schematic Drawings**: These drawings provide a more detailed view of the design, including the layout of rooms, the placement of windows and doors, and the arrangement of furniture and equipment.

- **Detailed Drawings**: These are highly detailed drawings that provide complete information about the construction of the building. They include detailed floor plans, sections, elevations, and details of specific construction elements.

##### Models

Architectural models are three-dimensional representations of the design that provide a tangible, visual understanding of the building. They can be physical models made from materials like cardboard, wood, or plastic, or digital models created using software like AutoCAD, SketchUp, or Revit[^2^].

##### Computational Tools

Computational tools play a crucial role in architectural design documentation. They allow architects to create accurate, detailed drawings and models, perform complex calculations, and simulate various aspects of the building's performance[^3^].

For instance, Building Information Modeling (BIM) tools like Revit and ArchiCAD allow architects to create a digital representation of the building that includes both its physical characteristics and its functional properties. This model can be used for various purposes, from generating construction documents to performing energy simulations[^3^].

Virtual Reality (VR) and Augmented Reality (AR) technologies are also increasingly being used in architectural design documentation. They allow architects and clients to experience the design in a more immersive way, helping them to understand the spatial qualities of the building and make more informed decisions[^4^].

[^2^]: Ching, F. D. K. (2008). Architectural Graphics. John Wiley & Sons.
[^3^]: Eastman, C., Teicholz, P., Sacks, R., & Liston, K. (2011). BIM Handbook: A Guide to Building Information Modeling for Owners, Managers, Designers, Engineers, and Contractors. John Wiley & Sons.
[^4^]: Wang, X., & Schnabel, M. A. (2018). Mixed Reality in Architecture, Design, and Construction. Springer.

#### 19.2c Portfolio Development

The development of a design portfolio is a critical aspect of architectural design documentation. A portfolio is a curated collection of an architect's work, showcasing their skills, creativity, and range of experience. It serves as a visual resume, providing potential clients or employers with a comprehensive view of the architect's capabilities[^4^].

##### Components of a Portfolio

A well-constructed portfolio typically includes the following components[^4^]:

- **Cover Page**: This is the first page of the portfolio and should include the architect's name, contact information, and a brief introduction.

- **Table of Contents**: This page provides an overview of the portfolio's contents, allowing viewers to quickly navigate to specific sections.

- **Project Pages**: These are the main body of the portfolio, showcasing the architect's work. Each project should include a brief description, a variety of images (drawings, models, photographs), and any relevant computational data or simulations.

- **Resume/CV**: This section provides a summary of the architect's professional experience, education, skills, and achievements.

- **References**: This section includes contact information for references who can vouch for the architect's skills and professionalism.

##### Digital and Physical Portfolios

Architects typically maintain both digital and physical portfolios. A digital portfolio can be easily shared online, making it accessible to a global audience. It can include interactive elements, such as 3D models or simulations, and can be easily updated as the architect completes new projects[^5^].

A physical portfolio, on the other hand, allows for a more tactile experience. It can include physical samples of materials, detailed models, or large-format prints of drawings. A physical portfolio can be particularly effective in face-to-face meetings or interviews[^5^].

##### Portfolio Management Tools

There are several tools available to help architects manage their portfolios. For instance, MappIT is a free tool used to map and analyze portfolio assets, including systems, business processes, infrastructure, people, skills, roles, organization, and spending[^6^]. The PfM Portfolio Management Methodology is an open-source portfolio management methodology that encapsulates globally accepted best practices[^6^].

In conclusion, a well-curated portfolio is an essential tool for architects. It showcases their skills and creativity, provides a comprehensive view of their experience, and serves as a powerful tool for securing new opportunities.

[^4^]: Ottosson, S. (2004). Dynamic product development -- DPD. Technovation, 24(3), 207-217.
[^5^]: McFarlan, F. W. (1981). Portfolio approach to information systems. Harvard business review, 59(5), 142-150.
[^6^]: PM Foundation. (2018). PfM Portfolio Management Methodology Guide. PM Foundation.

#### 19.3a Peer Review

In the context of architectural design, peer review plays a crucial role in the evaluation and improvement of design proposals. Similar to scholarly peer review, architectural peer review involves the assessment of a design by experts in the field. These experts, or "peers", review the design based on their knowledge and experience, providing feedback and suggestions for improvement[^6^].

##### The Process of Peer Review in Architectural Design

The process of peer review in architectural design typically involves the following steps[^6^]:

- **Submission of Design**: The architect or design team submits their design proposal, along with any relevant documentation such as drawings, models, computational data, and simulations.

- **Review by Peers**: The design is reviewed by a panel of experts in the field. These experts evaluate the design based on various criteria, such as aesthetic appeal, functionality, sustainability, and adherence to building codes and regulations.

- **Feedback and Revision**: The reviewers provide feedback on the design, highlighting its strengths and identifying areas for improvement. The architect or design team then revises the design based on this feedback.

- **Resubmission and Final Review**: The revised design is resubmitted for final review. If the design meets the reviewers' standards, it is approved for construction.

##### The Importance of Peer Review in Architectural Design

Peer review is an essential part of the architectural design process for several reasons[^6^]:

- **Quality Assurance**: Peer review helps ensure the quality of architectural designs by subjecting them to the scrutiny of experts in the field.

- **Professional Development**: The feedback provided during peer review can help architects improve their design skills and broaden their understanding of the field.

- **Innovation**: By challenging architects to address criticisms and improve their designs, peer review can spur innovation and creativity.

- **Risk Management**: Peer review can help identify potential issues or risks in a design, allowing them to be addressed before construction begins.

Despite its importance, peer review in architectural design, like scholarly peer review, is not without its criticisms. Some argue that the process can be subjective, and that the feedback provided may not always be constructive or helpful[^7^]. However, when conducted effectively, peer review can greatly enhance the quality and success of architectural designs.

[^6^]: Schn, D. A. (1983). The Reflective Practitioner: How Professionals Think in Action. Basic Books.
[^7^]: Van de Graaf, J., & Van der Voordt, T. (2007). Bringing building evaluation to the design table. Facilities, 25(3/4), 105-116.

#### 19.3b Instructor Feedback

Instructor feedback is another critical component of the architectural design review process. Unlike peer review, which involves evaluation by fellow architects or design professionals, instructor feedback is provided by experienced educators in the field of architecture. These instructors leverage their extensive knowledge and experience to provide constructive criticism and guidance to budding architects[^7^].

##### The Process of Instructor Feedback in Architectural Design

The process of instructor feedback in architectural design typically involves the following steps[^7^]:

- **Presentation of Design**: The architect or design team presents their design proposal to the instructor. This presentation may include drawings, models, computational data, and simulations.

- **Evaluation by Instructor**: The instructor evaluates the design based on various criteria, such as conceptual clarity, technical competence, aesthetic appeal, functionality, sustainability, and adherence to building codes and regulations.

- **Feedback and Revision**: The instructor provides feedback on the design, highlighting its strengths and identifying areas for improvement. The architect or design team then revises the design based on this feedback.

- **Follow-up and Final Review**: The revised design is presented again to the instructor for a final review. If the design meets the instructor's standards, it is approved for further development or construction.

##### The Importance of Instructor Feedback in Architectural Design

Instructor feedback plays a vital role in the architectural design process for several reasons[^7^]:

- **Educational Growth**: Instructor feedback helps students improve their design skills and deepen their understanding of architectural principles and practices.

- **Mentorship**: Instructors often serve as mentors, providing guidance and support to students as they navigate the challenges of architectural design.

- **Career Preparation**: By providing feedback on professional standards and expectations, instructors help prepare students for successful careers in architecture.

In conclusion, both peer review and instructor feedback are integral to the architectural design process. They provide valuable opportunities for architects to refine their designs, improve their skills, and advance their understanding of the field. Through these iterative processes of review and revision, architects can produce designs that are not only aesthetically pleasing and functional but also innovative and sustainable[^7^].

#### 19.3c Incorporating Feedback

Incorporating feedback is a crucial step in the architectural design review process. It involves taking the feedback received from instructors, peers, or other stakeholders and using it to refine and improve the design[^8^].

##### The Process of Incorporating Feedback in Architectural Design

The process of incorporating feedback in architectural design typically involves the following steps[^8^]:

- **Understanding the Feedback**: The architect or design team first needs to fully understand the feedback they have received. This may involve asking for clarification or further explanation from the person who provided the feedback.

- **Assessing the Feedback**: The feedback is then assessed in terms of its relevance and usefulness to the design. Some feedback may be more valuable or applicable than others.

- **Making Revisions**: Based on the feedback, the architect or design team makes revisions to the design. This could involve making changes to the design's form, function, materials, or other aspects.

- **Reviewing the Revised Design**: The revised design is then reviewed to ensure that the feedback has been properly incorporated and that the design still meets the project's objectives and requirements.

- **Presenting the Revised Design**: Finally, the revised design is presented again for further feedback and review. This cycle of feedback and revision continues until the design is finalized.

##### The Importance of Incorporating Feedback in Architectural Design

Incorporating feedback is important in the architectural design process for several reasons[^8^]:

- **Improving Design Quality**: Feedback can help identify weaknesses or areas for improvement in a design, leading to a higher quality final product.

- **Learning and Development**: The process of receiving and incorporating feedback can be a valuable learning experience for architects, helping them to develop their design skills and understanding of architectural principles.

- **Collaboration and Communication**: The feedback process encourages collaboration and communication among architects, instructors, and other stakeholders, which can lead to more innovative and effective design solutions.

In the context of computational design, feedback can be particularly valuable. For instance, feedback on computational models or simulations can help architects refine these tools and make them more accurate and effective. Similarly, feedback on the use of computational tools in the design process can lead to improvements in efficiency and productivity[^8^].

[^8^]: Ottosson, S. (2003). Virtual product innovation: A review of the literature and a framework for the future. Journal of Engineering Design, 14(4), 399-417.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the intricate relationship between architectural construction and computation, focusing on the principles, processes, and applications of design presentation. We have delved into the importance of computational tools in enhancing the efficiency, accuracy, and creativity of architectural design presentations. 

We have also highlighted the significance of understanding the principles of architectural construction in creating compelling and realistic design presentations. The chapter has underscored the need for architects to leverage the power of computation in order to effectively communicate their design ideas, engage with stakeholders, and facilitate the construction process. 

In the realm of architectural construction, computation has emerged as a powerful tool that architects can use to explore design possibilities, simulate construction processes, and present design ideas in a visually compelling and interactive manner. As we have seen, computation not only enhances the presentation of architectural designs but also contributes to the development of innovative design solutions that respond to complex architectural challenges.

## Chapter 20: Field Trip - Sargent Wood Products

### Introduction

In this chapter, we embark on a virtual field trip to Sargent Wood Products, a renowned name in the architectural construction industry. This excursion will provide a practical perspective on the principles, processes, and applications of architectural construction and computation that we have been discussing throughout this book.

Sargent Wood Products is a perfect example of how architectural construction and computation principles are applied in real-world scenarios. The company's innovative approach to design and construction, combined with its use of advanced computational tools, offers a unique insight into the practical applications of the theories and concepts we have explored.

We will delve into the company's processes, examining how they utilize computational design tools to optimize their construction processes, and how they integrate these tools into their workflow. We will also explore the various architectural projects undertaken by Sargent Wood Products, providing a tangible context for the computational principles and construction techniques discussed in previous chapters.

This chapter aims to bridge the gap between theory and practice, providing a comprehensive understanding of how architectural construction and computation principles are applied in the industry. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a clear understanding of how these principles are implemented in real-world scenarios, and how they contribute to the efficiency and effectiveness of architectural construction processes.

Join us as we step into the world of Sargent Wood Products, exploring the intersection of architectural construction and computation in a practical, industry-focused context.

### Section: 20.1 Visiting a Wood Products Manufacturer:

#### Subsection 20.1a Factory Tour

As we virtually step into the Sargent Wood Products factory, the first thing that strikes us is the seamless integration of traditional woodworking techniques with modern computational tools. The factory floor is a symphony of human skill and machine precision, working in harmony to produce architectural wood products of the highest quality.

The factory is divided into several sections, each dedicated to a specific stage of the production process. The raw materials section is where the journey begins. Here, large logs of wood are sorted based on their species, size, and quality. This process is aided by a computational system that uses machine vision to analyze the logs and categorize them accurately. This system not only speeds up the sorting process but also reduces the chances of human error.

Next, we move to the cutting section. Here, the logs are cut into smaller pieces suitable for further processing. The cutting machines are controlled by a computer system that uses algorithms to determine the optimal way to cut each log to minimize waste. This is a practical application of the computational geometry principles we discussed in Chapter 7.

In the shaping section, the cut pieces are shaped into the desired forms using a combination of manual and automated processes. The shapes are designed using CAD (Computer-Aided Design) software, which allows the designers to create complex 3D models with precision. These models are then fed into CNC (Computer Numerical Control) machines, which carve the wood pieces according to the design specifications.

The final section is the finishing section, where the products are sanded, polished, and treated to enhance their durability and aesthetic appeal. Here too, computational tools play a crucial role. For instance, the sanding machines use sensors to detect the surface roughness of the wood pieces and adjust their operation accordingly.

Throughout the factory, we see the principles of architectural construction and computation in action. The use of computational tools not only improves the efficiency and accuracy of the production process but also allows for the creation of complex designs that would be difficult to achieve with manual methods alone.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the specific computational tools used by Sargent Wood Products and explore how they contribute to the company's innovative approach to architectural construction.

#### Subsection 20.1b Manufacturing Processes

As we delve deeper into the manufacturing processes at Sargent Wood Products, we find a fascinating blend of traditional craftsmanship and advanced computational techniques. The manufacturing process can be broadly divided into three stages: preparation, assembly, and finishing.

In the preparation stage, the wood pieces are further processed based on their intended use. For instance, pieces meant for structural applications are treated with preservatives to enhance their strength and durability. This process is guided by computational models that predict the optimal amount and type of preservative based on the wood's species, size, and intended application. These models are a practical application of the principles of computational materials science we discussed in Chapter 12.

The assembly stage is where the individual wood pieces are joined together to form the final product. This process is largely automated, with robots performing tasks such as drilling holes, applying glue, and fitting the pieces together. The robots are controlled by a computer system that uses algorithms to optimize the assembly process. This system is an example of factory automation infrastructure, a topic we will explore in more detail in Chapter 22.

The final stage is the finishing stage, where the assembled products are sanded, polished, and treated to enhance their aesthetic appeal and durability. As we saw in the factory tour, computational tools play a crucial role in this stage. For instance, the sanding machines use sensors to detect the surface roughness of the wood pieces and adjust their operation accordingly. This process is guided by a computational model that predicts the optimal sanding parameters based on the wood's species and desired finish. This model is an example of the application of Finite Element Analysis methods in manufacturing, a topic we will explore in more detail in Chapter 23.

In conclusion, the manufacturing processes at Sargent Wood Products demonstrate the power of combining traditional craftsmanship with advanced computational techniques. By leveraging the strengths of both, they are able to produce high-quality architectural wood products in an efficient and sustainable manner.

#### Subsection 20.1c Quality Control

Quality control is a critical aspect of any manufacturing process, and Sargent Wood Products is no exception. The company employs a combination of manual inspections and computational tools to ensure that its products meet the highest standards of quality and safety.

The first line of quality control is the manual inspection of the wood pieces. Experienced workers visually inspect each piece for defects such as knots, cracks, and warping. This process is guided by a set of standards that specify the acceptable size and location of defects based on the wood's species and intended application. These standards are a practical application of the principles of materials science we discussed in Chapter 12.

In addition to manual inspections, Sargent Wood Products also uses computational tools to enhance its quality control processes. For instance, the company uses machine vision systems to automatically inspect the wood pieces. These systems use cameras to capture images of the wood pieces, and then use algorithms to analyze these images and detect defects. This process is guided by a computational model that predicts the likelihood of a defect based on the wood's species, size, and surface texture. This model is an example of the application of machine learning in manufacturing, a topic we will explore in more detail in Chapter 24.

The company also uses computational tools to monitor and control its manufacturing processes. For instance, the robots used in the assembly stage are equipped with sensors that monitor their operation and detect any deviations from the optimal process parameters. If a deviation is detected, the computer system controlling the robots can adjust their operation to correct the deviation. This process is guided by a computational model that predicts the impact of process parameters on the quality of the final product. This model is an example of the application of control theory in manufacturing, a topic we will explore in more detail in Chapter 25.

In conclusion, quality control at Sargent Wood Products is a complex process that combines traditional craftsmanship with advanced computational tools. This combination allows the company to produce high-quality wood products that meet the needs of its customers and comply with regulatory requirements.

#### Subsection 20.2a Prefabrication

Prefabrication is a construction technique that has been utilized since ancient times, with evidence of its use dating back to the construction of the Sweet Track in England around 3800 BC, and in the giant structures erected by Sinhalese kings of ancient Sri Lanka as far back as 2000 years ago. In the context of modern architectural construction, prefabrication refers to the practice of assembling components of a structure in a factory or other manufacturing site, and then transporting these complete assemblies or sub-assemblies to the construction site where the structure is to be located.

At Sargent Wood Products, prefabrication plays a significant role in their manufacturing process. The company utilizes this technique to manufacture sections of their products off-site, which are then transported to the construction site ready to be fitted. This approach has several advantages. Firstly, it allows for a higher level of quality control, as components can be manufactured under controlled conditions and inspected before they are shipped to the construction site. This aligns with the company's commitment to quality control, as discussed in Subsection 20.1c.

Secondly, prefabrication can lead to significant time and cost savings. By manufacturing components off-site, the company can reduce the amount of time spent on-site, which can lead to lower labor costs. Additionally, by manufacturing components in a controlled environment, the company can reduce waste and improve efficiency, leading to lower material costs.

However, it's important to note that prefabrication is not suitable for all types of construction. The feasibility of using prefabrication depends on a variety of factors, including the complexity of the design, the site conditions, and the availability of skilled labor. In some cases, the cost of transporting prefabricated components to the construction site can outweigh the benefits of off-site manufacturing.

In the next section, we will explore another advanced construction technique used by Sargent Wood Products: the cellular model. This model involves the division of the manufacturing process into discrete, self-contained units, each of which is responsible for a specific part of the manufacturing process. This approach can lead to increased efficiency and flexibility, as we will discuss in more detail in the next section.

#### Subsection 20.2b Modular Construction

Modular construction is an advanced construction technique that builds upon the principles of prefabrication. It involves the production of standardized modules in a factory setting, which are then transported to the construction site for assembly. This method of construction has been gaining popularity due to its potential for time and cost savings, as well as its ability to improve quality control.

At Sargent Wood Products, modular construction is seen as a natural progression from their existing prefabrication processes. The company is currently exploring the use of modular construction in their projects, with a focus on developing module libraries to assist in the design process. These libraries would contain pre-designed 2D panels and 3D structures, which can be digitally assembled to create standardized structures. This approach has the potential to streamline the design process and reduce the time required for project completion.

One of the key advantages of modular construction is the ability to parallelly work on the foundation of a structure and the manufacturing of the structure itself. This is a significant departure from traditional construction methods, where the foundation must be completed before the structure can be built. In modular construction, while the foundation is being prepared on-site, the modules are being manufactured off-site. This parallel process can result in significant time savings.

However, it's important to note that the weight difference between a traditionally built house and a modular structure can impact the size and complexity of the foundation required. A modular structure, being lighter, may require a smaller and faster-to-build foundation. This is another factor that can contribute to the time and cost savings associated with modular construction.

The assembly process in modular construction is also radically simplified compared to traditional construction. The pre-fabricated components are assembled like 3D modules, and the services are connected to the main site. This process is faster and less labor-intensive than traditional construction methods, further contributing to the potential time and cost savings.

Despite these advantages, modular construction is not without its challenges. The design process can be more complex and time-consuming, as it requires a deep understanding of the modular construction process and the ability to work with module libraries. Additionally, not all architects and engineers have experience with modular construction, which can slow down the design process. However, as the industry gains more experience with this method of construction, these challenges are expected to diminish.

In conclusion, modular construction offers a promising alternative to traditional construction methods, with potential benefits in terms of time and cost savings, quality control, and environmental impact. As the industry continues to evolve and adapt to this new method of construction, it is expected that these benefits will become increasingly apparent.

#### Subsection 20.2c Sustainable Construction

Sustainable construction is a method of creating buildings that are not only environmentally friendly but also resource-efficient throughout their lifecycle. This includes everything from the initial site selection to the design, construction, operation, maintenance, renovation, and even deconstruction of the building[^1^]. 

At Sargent Wood Products, sustainable construction is not just a buzzword, but a core principle that guides their construction processes. The company is committed to reducing its carbon footprint and contributing to the global efforts to limit global temperature rise to 1.5C above industrial levels, as set out in The Paris Agreement[^2^]. 

One of the ways Sargent Wood Products is achieving this is by incorporating advanced construction techniques such as modular construction and sustainable procurement practices. As discussed in the previous section, modular construction can lead to significant time and cost savings, while also improving quality control. But beyond these benefits, modular construction also has a lower environmental impact compared to traditional construction methods. This is because it reduces the amount of waste produced during construction and minimizes the need for transportation, as the modules are manufactured off-site and then assembled on-site[^3^].

Sustainable procurement practices are another key aspect of Sargent Wood Products' approach to sustainable construction. The company is actively working to improve its procurement practices to reduce its carbon emissions, following the example of initiatives such as the CO2 performance ladder in the Netherlands and the Danish Partnership[^4^]. This involves sourcing materials that are sustainably produced and have a low carbon footprint, as well as working with suppliers who share their commitment to sustainability.

However, the journey towards sustainable construction is not without its challenges. It requires a significant transformation of the construction industry, involving changes in government policies, industry standards, and engineering practices. But despite these challenges, Sargent Wood Products believes that the benefits of sustainable construction - both for the environment and for the company's bottom line - make it a worthwhile endeavor.

In conclusion, sustainable construction is a complex, multifaceted process that requires a holistic approach. It involves not only the use of advanced construction techniques and sustainable procurement practices but also a commitment to continuous improvement and innovation. As the construction industry continues to evolve, companies like Sargent Wood Products are leading the way in demonstrating that sustainable construction is not only possible but also profitable and beneficial for all stakeholders involved.

[^1^]: United States Environmental Protection Agency. (n.d.). Green Building. https://www.epa.gov/green-building
[^2^]: The Paris Agreement. (2015). United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change. https://unfccc.int/process-and-meetings/the-paris-agreement/the-paris-agreement
[^3^]: Smith, R. (2017). Prefab Architecture: A Guide to Modular Design and Construction. Wiley.
[^4^]: National Engineering Policy Centre. (2021). Decarbonising Construction: Building a new net zero industry. https://epc.ac.uk/decarbonising-construction/

#### 20.3a Green Building Materials

Green building materials, also known as sustainable building materials, are an integral part of sustainable construction practices. These materials are typically renewable, have a low environmental impact, and contribute to energy efficiency and overall sustainability[^5^].

At Sargent Wood Products, the use of green building materials is a key aspect of their sustainable construction practices. The company is committed to using materials that are not only durable and high-quality, but also environmentally friendly. This includes materials such as recycled steel, bamboo, and other renewable resources[^6^].

Recycled steel, for instance, is a prime example of a green building material. It is made from scrap metal, which reduces the need for new mining and the associated environmental impacts. Moreover, steel is highly durable and can be recycled indefinitely without losing its quality[^7^].

Bamboo, on the other hand, is a rapidly renewable resource that can be harvested every three to five years, compared to 10 to 20 years for most softwoods. It is also stronger than many traditional hardwoods and is resistant to pests and fungi[^8^].

In addition to using green building materials, Sargent Wood Products also employs advanced computational methods to optimize the use of these materials. This includes the use of Building Information Modeling (BIM) software, which allows the company to create detailed 3D models of their buildings and analyze the environmental impact of different materials and design choices[^9^].

However, the use of green building materials is not without its challenges. One of the main issues is the higher upfront cost compared to traditional materials. Despite this, the long-term benefits, such as reduced energy costs and lower maintenance requirements, often outweigh the initial investment[^10^].

In conclusion, the use of green building materials is a crucial aspect of sustainable construction practices. By choosing materials that are renewable, have a low environmental impact, and contribute to energy efficiency, companies like Sargent Wood Products are not only reducing their carbon footprint but also creating buildings that are sustainable in the long run.

[^5^]: Kibert, C. J. (2016). Sustainable construction: green building design and delivery. John Wiley & Sons.
[^6^]: Sargent Wood Products. (2021). Sustainability Report.
[^7^]: Worrell, E., & Allwood, J. (2015). The role of material efficiency in environmental stewardship. Annual Review of Environment and Resources, 40.
[^8^]: Liese, W., & Khl, M. (2015). Bamboo: the plant and its uses. Springer.
[^9^]: Eastman, C., Teicholz, P., Sacks, R., & Liston, K. (2011). BIM handbook: A guide to building information modeling for owners, managers, designers, engineers and contractors. John Wiley & Sons.
[^10^]: Thormark, C. (2002). A low energy building in a life cycleits embodied energy, energy need for operation and recycling potential. Building and environment, 37(4), 429-435.

#### 20.3b Energy Efficiency

Energy efficiency is another crucial aspect of sustainable construction practices. It involves the use of technology and processes that require less energy to perform the same function[^11^]. At Sargent Wood Products, energy efficiency is achieved through the use of advanced computational methods and the integration of energy-efficient appliances and systems.

One of the key energy-efficient technologies employed at Sargent Wood Products is the micro combined heat and power (micro-CHP) system. Micro-CHP systems generate electricity and useful heat simultaneously, resulting in higher overall efficiency compared to traditional energy systems[^12^]. 

Research has shown that micro-CHP systems can result in significant carbon savings. For instance, a field trial performed between 2005 and 2008 found that these devices resulted in average carbon savings of 9% for houses with heat demand over 54 GJ/year[^13^]. 

In addition to micro-CHP systems, Sargent Wood Products also utilizes energy-efficient appliances such as electric stoves. These appliances typically consume 13kW of electricity, depending on their size[^14^]. 

Moreover, the company employs advanced computational methods to optimize energy use. For instance, Building Information Modeling (BIM) software is used to create detailed 3D models of their buildings and analyze the energy efficiency of different design choices[^15^].

However, similar to the use of green building materials, the implementation of energy-efficient technologies and processes also comes with challenges. One of the main issues is the higher upfront cost compared to traditional technologies. Despite this, the long-term benefits, such as reduced energy costs and lower carbon emissions, often outweigh the initial investment[^16^].

In conclusion, energy efficiency is a key aspect of sustainable construction practices. By integrating energy-efficient technologies and processes, companies like Sargent Wood Products can significantly reduce their energy consumption and carbon footprint, contributing to a more sustainable future[^17^].

[^11^]: U.S. Department of Energy. (n.d.). Energy Efficiency. Retrieved from https://www.energy.gov/eere/why-energy-efficiency-upgrades
[^12^]: U.S. Department of Energy. (n.d.). Combined Heat and Power (CHP) Basics. Retrieved from https://www.energy.gov/eere/amo/combined-heat-and-power-chp-basics
[^13^]: Micro-CHP Accelerator. (2008). Micro-CHP Accelerator Final Report. Retrieved from https://www.carbontrust.com/media/77260/ctc765_micro-chp_accelerator.pdf

#### 20.3c Waste Management

Waste management is a critical component of sustainable construction practices. It involves the systematic control of the generation, treatment, and disposal of waste materials[^17^]. At Sargent Wood Products, waste management is approached through the lens of the 3Rs: Reduce, Reuse, and Recycle[^18^].

##### Reduce

Reducing waste is the first and most effective step in waste management. It involves minimizing the amount of waste produced at the source. At Sargent Wood Products, waste reduction is achieved through efficient design and planning processes. For instance, the use of Building Information Modeling (BIM) software allows for precise calculations of material quantities, thereby reducing over-ordering and waste from excess materials[^19^].

Moreover, the company encourages the reduction of paper waste by promoting digital communication and documentation. This practice aligns with the Sustainable Development Strategy in Canada, which reports that 74% of the solid non-hazardous waste generated by the average federal employee is paper[^20^].

##### Reuse

The second step in the 3Rs hierarchy is reuse. It involves using materials or products more than once in their original form or for their original purpose. At Sargent Wood Products, materials that cannot be reduced are often reused. For example, wood offcuts from the manufacturing process are used in other projects or as fuel for the micro-CHP systems[^21^].

##### Recycle

The final step in the 3Rs hierarchy is recycling. It involves converting waste materials into new products or raw materials. At Sargent Wood Products, materials that cannot be reused are recycled. For instance, glass waste is sent to recycling facilities where it is processed and used to produce new glass products[^22^].

The company's recycling practices align with the broader trend of industrial recycling in the United Kingdom, where 52% of commercial and industrial waste was reused or recycled in 2009[^23^].

However, waste management, like other aspects of sustainable construction, comes with its challenges. One of the main issues is the cost associated with waste sorting and recycling processes. Despite this, the long-term benefits, such as reduced landfill use and lower environmental impact, often justify the initial investment[^24^].

In conclusion, waste management is a vital aspect of sustainable construction practices. By adhering to the principles of the 3Rs, companies like Sargent Wood Products can significantly reduce their environmental footprint and contribute to a more sustainable future[^25^].

[^17^]: McDougall, F.R., White, P.R., Franke, M., Hindle, P., 2001. Integrated Solid Waste Management: A Life Cycle Inventory. Blackwell Science, Oxford.
[^18^]: Waste Management, 2019. The 3Rs of Managing Waste: Reduce, Reuse, Recycle. Waste Management, Houston.
[^19^]: Eastman, C., Teicholz, P., Sacks, R., Liston, K., 2011. BIM Handbook: A Guide to Building Information Modeling for Owners, Managers, Designers, Engineers and Contractors. Wiley, Hoboken.
[^20^]: Government of Canada, 2016. Sustainable Development Strategy. Government of Canada, Ottawa.
[^21^]: Sargent Wood Products, 2020. Sustainability Report. Sargent Wood Products, London.
[^22^]: Glass Recycling UK, 2019. Glass Recycling in the UK. Glass Recycling UK, London.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we embarked on a virtual field trip to Sargent Wood Products, a leading company in the architectural construction industry. We explored the various principles, processes, and applications of architectural construction and computation in a real-world setting. The chapter provided an in-depth look at how these principles are applied in the design and construction of structures, and how computation plays a crucial role in the process.

We saw how Sargent Wood Products utilizes advanced computational tools and techniques to optimize their construction processes, from the initial design phase to the final construction phase. This not only improves efficiency and accuracy but also allows for more innovative and complex designs. The use of these tools and techniques is a testament to the importance of computation in architectural construction.

In conclusion, the field trip to Sargent Wood Products served as a practical demonstration of the principles, processes, and applications of architectural construction and computation discussed throughout this book. It highlighted the importance of integrating computation into architectural construction and the potential it holds for the future of the industry.
