## Chapter 21: Presentation on Construction

### Introduction

The art of architectural construction is a complex and intricate process that requires a deep understanding of both the principles of design and the practicalities of building. This chapter, "Presentation on Construction", aims to provide a comprehensive overview of the various aspects involved in the construction process, from the initial design phase to the final completion of the project.

In the realm of architecture, the presentation of construction is not merely about showcasing the final product. It is about illustrating the journey of the project, the decisions made, the challenges faced, and the solutions found. It is about demonstrating the thought processes, the technical expertise, and the creative ingenuity that went into the creation of the structure.

This chapter will delve into the various methods and techniques used in the presentation of construction, from traditional hand-drawn plans and models to modern digital renderings and simulations. It will explore the role of computation in the construction process, and how it has revolutionized the way architects design, plan, and execute their projects.

The chapter will also discuss the importance of effective communication in the construction process, and how architects can use their presentations to convey their ideas, their vision, and their understanding of the project to their clients, their team, and the wider public.

Whether you are an experienced architect looking to refine your presentation skills, or a student just starting out in the field, this chapter will provide you with valuable insights into the art and science of presenting construction. It will equip you with the knowledge and tools you need to effectively communicate your ideas and showcase your work in a way that is engaging, informative, and inspiring.

### Section: 21.1 Construction Presentations:

#### 21.1a Presentation Preparation

The preparation of a construction presentation is a critical step in the architectural process. It is during this stage that architects consolidate their ideas, their designs, and their plans into a coherent and compelling narrative. This narrative not only showcases the final product but also the journey of the project, the decisions made, the challenges faced, and the solutions found.

The preparation process begins with the collection and organization of all relevant information. This includes design sketches, architectural plans, construction details, material specifications, and any other data that is pertinent to the project. This information is then analyzed and synthesized into a clear and concise presentation.

The next step is to decide on the format of the presentation. Traditional methods include hand-drawn plans and physical models, while modern methods involve digital renderings and simulations. The choice of format will depend on a variety of factors, including the nature of the project, the audience, and the architect's personal style and preferences.

In recent years, computation has become an increasingly important tool in the preparation of construction presentations. Software programs such as Autodesk's AutoCAD, Revit, and 3ds Max, as well as Adobe's Photoshop and After Effects, have revolutionized the way architects visualize and present their designs. These tools allow for a level of precision, detail, and realism that was previously unattainable.

However, it is important to remember that the purpose of a construction presentation is not just to impress with technical prowess or artistic flair. The ultimate goal is to communicate effectively. This means presenting information in a way that is clear, understandable, and engaging. It means telling a story that captures the essence of the project and conveys the architect's vision.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the various aspects of construction presentations, including the use of computational tools, the principles of effective communication, and the role of presentations in the broader context of architectural construction.

#### 21.1b Presentation Techniques

In the realm of architectural construction presentations, a variety of techniques can be employed to effectively communicate the architect's vision. These techniques can be broadly categorized into two groups: traditional and digital.

Traditional presentation techniques include hand-drawn sketches, physical models, and printed plans. These methods, while seemingly old-fashioned, still hold significant value in today's digital age. Hand-drawn sketches, for instance, can convey the initial concept and the creative process behind a design. Physical models, on the other hand, provide a tangible representation of the project, allowing viewers to understand the spatial relationships and proportions in a way that digital renderings sometimes fail to capture.

Digital presentation techniques, however, have become increasingly prevalent due to their versatility and efficiency. These techniques utilize software tools to create detailed renderings, animations, and interactive models. Autodesk's suite of software, including AutoCAD, Revit, and 3ds Max, is commonly used for creating architectural drawings and 3D models. Adobe's Photoshop and After Effects are often employed for post-production, enhancing renderings and creating compelling visual narratives.

One emerging trend in digital presentations is the use of multimodal interaction, which involves the use of multiple modes of communication to enhance the user's experience. This can include the integration of text, images, audio, and video, as well as interactive elements such as virtual reality (VR) and augmented reality (AR). These technologies can provide immersive experiences, allowing viewers to explore the project in a virtual environment.

Regardless of the techniques used, the key to a successful construction presentation lies in its ability to effectively communicate the architect's vision. This involves not only showcasing the final design but also illustrating the process behind it. It requires a careful balance between technical detail and artistic expression, between precision and creativity.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the role of computation in architectural construction presentations, exploring how it can be utilized to enhance both the creation and the delivery of these presentations.

#### 21.1c Audience Engagement

Engaging the audience is a crucial aspect of any presentation, and architectural construction presentations are no exception. The goal is to not only present the architectural design and construction process but also to captivate the audience's attention, stimulate their interest, and encourage their participation. This can be achieved through a combination of effective communication, interactive elements, and audience response systems.

Effective communication involves presenting the information in a clear, concise, and compelling manner. This can be achieved through the use of visual aids, storytelling techniques, and a well-structured narrative. The presenter should also be able to adapt their presentation style to suit the audience's level of understanding and interest.

Interactive elements can significantly enhance the audience's engagement. As discussed in the previous section, multimodal interaction, which involves the use of multiple modes of communication, can provide an immersive experience. This can include the integration of text, images, audio, and video, as well as interactive elements such as virtual reality (VR) and augmented reality (AR). These technologies allow the audience to explore the project in a virtual environment, thereby enhancing their understanding and appreciation of the design.

Audience response systems can also be used to engage the audience and gather their feedback. These systems work with the participants' existing computing devices, such as notebook computers, smartphones, and PDAs, which are typically connected to the Internet via Wi-Fi. The facilitator's computer controls a set of web pages presenting questions, and the participants log into the server using web browsers to see the questions and input their responses. The summarized responses can be displayed through the projector and also on each participant's device. This allows the presenter to gauge the audience's understanding and adjust the presentation accordingly.

The Internet has made it possible to gather audience responses on a massive scale. For example, during the 2013 State of The Union (US) address by president Barack Obama, Microsoft featured Bing Pulse, which allowed registered users to input their responses (positive, negative, neutral) to the address and visualized the results as a trending graph in real time. This concept has been used in various other contexts, including national news broadcasts and live meetings.

In conclusion, audience engagement is a critical component of architectural construction presentations. By effectively communicating the information, incorporating interactive elements, and utilizing audience response systems, presenters can ensure that their audience is not only informed but also actively engaged.

#### 21.2a Construction Drawings

Construction drawings, also known as plans or blueprints, are a fundamental part of architectural construction documentation. They provide a detailed visual representation of the proposed structure, offering a comprehensive guide for the construction team. These drawings are typically produced by architects and engineers, using computer-aided design (CAD) software, and are based on the design concept approved by the client.

Construction drawings include a variety of different types of plans, each serving a specific purpose. These include:

1. **Site Plans:** These provide an overview of the entire project site, showing the location of the proposed structure in relation to existing structures, roads, and other site features. They also indicate the orientation of the building and may include information about site topography and landscaping.

2. **Floor Plans:** These are horizontal views of each floor of the building, showing the layout of rooms, corridors, and other spaces. They also indicate the location of doors, windows, and built-in features such as cabinets and appliances.

3. **Elevations:** These are vertical views of the building, showing the exterior appearance and indicating the height of various features. There are typically four elevations (front, rear, and sides), but more may be provided for complex structures.

4. **Sections:** These are vertical cuts through the building, showing the internal structure and providing information about construction methods and materials. They also indicate the relationship between different levels of the building.

5. **Details:** These are close-up views of specific construction elements, providing detailed information about materials, assembly methods, and dimensions. They are typically used for complex or unique features that require special attention during construction.

6. **Schedules:** These are tables listing various elements of the building, such as doors, windows, and finishes, along with their specifications. They serve as a quick reference for the construction team.

The construction drawings should be as clear and complete as possible, providing all the information needed to construct the building as designed. They should also be consistent with the specifications and other construction documents. Any changes or alterations from the original documents should be clearly noted and approved by the architect and engineer.

In addition to the traditional 2D drawings, many architects and engineers now use Building Information Modeling (BIM) software to create 3D models of the proposed structure. These models provide a more realistic and comprehensive view of the building, allowing the construction team to better understand the design and identify potential issues before construction begins. They can also be used to generate the 2D drawings needed for construction.

In the next section, we will discuss the other key component of construction documentation: the specifications.

#### 21.2b Specifications

Specifications are an integral part of construction documentation, providing detailed written instructions that supplement the information provided in the construction drawings. They describe the materials, workmanship, and products that will be used in the construction process, as well as the standards and codes that must be adhered to. 

Specifications are typically prepared by the architect, often with input from engineers and other specialists. They are usually organized according to the Construction Specifications Institute's (CSI) MasterFormat, a standard system for organizing specifications and other written information for commercial and institutional building projects in the U.S. and Canada.

There are three main types of specifications:

1. **Prescriptive Specifications:** These provide detailed descriptions of the materials and workmanship required for a project. They specify the type, size, color, and other characteristics of each material, as well as the methods to be used in their installation. Prescriptive specifications are often used when the architect wants to control the exact nature of the finished work.

2. **Performance Specifications:** These describe the desired outcome of a construction element, but leave the choice of materials and methods up to the contractor. They specify the functional requirements that the finished work must meet, such as strength, durability, or energy efficiency. Performance specifications are often used when the architect is more concerned with the end result than with the specific means of achieving it.

3. **Proprietary Specifications:** These specify the use of specific products or systems, often by brand name. They may also include installation instructions provided by the manufacturer. Proprietary specifications are often used when a specific product has been preselected due to its unique characteristics or proven performance.

In addition to these, there are also **Reference Standard Specifications** which refer to a standard set by an authoritative body like ASTM or ANSI. These are used when a widely accepted industry standard can define the product or procedure.

Specifications are a critical part of the construction process, as they help to ensure that the finished building meets the design intent and the client's expectations. They also provide a basis for evaluating bids from contractors and for assessing the quality of the completed work. Therefore, they must be written clearly and accurately, with sufficient detail to avoid ambiguity and misunderstanding.

#### 21.2c As-Built Documentation

As-built documentation, also known as record drawings or built drawings, is a crucial part of the construction documentation process. These documents provide a comprehensive record of the final 'as-constructed' status of the project. They are typically prepared by the contractor, subcontractors, or the architect, and are based on the original design drawings and specifications, but include any changes made during the construction process.

As-built documentation serves several important purposes:

1. **Verification:** As-built documentation verifies that the construction has been completed according to the design and specifications. It provides a record of the actual materials used, the methods of installation, and the final dimensions and locations of all elements of the construction.

2. **Maintenance and Operations:** For the building owner and facilities management team, as-built documentation is a valuable resource for understanding the building's systems and components. This can assist in planning for maintenance, repairs, and upgrades.

3. **Future Renovations or Additions:** If the building is renovated or expanded in the future, as-built documentation can provide crucial information about the existing conditions. This can help to avoid conflicts and errors in the new design and construction.

4. **Legal and Regulatory Compliance:** In some jurisdictions, as-built documentation may be required for legal or regulatory compliance. It can also be useful in resolving disputes or claims related to the construction.

As-built documentation should be accurate, comprehensive, and clearly organized. It should include all relevant drawings, specifications, and other documents, with any changes clearly marked and explained. The use of digital technologies, such as Building Information Modeling (BIM), can greatly facilitate the creation and management of as-built documentation.

In conclusion, as-built documentation is a critical component of construction documentation, serving as a final record of the construction process and a valuable resource for building owners and operators. It is essential that these documents are prepared with care and precision to ensure their accuracy and usefulness.

#### 21.3a Peer Review

In the context of architectural construction, peer review plays a crucial role in ensuring the quality and integrity of the construction process. Similar to scholarly peer review, construction peer review involves the evaluation of a project's design, methods, and outcomes by experts in the field. This process can provide valuable feedback and insights, helping to identify potential issues and areas for improvement.

The peer review process in construction typically involves the following steps:

1. **Submission:** The architect or construction team submits the design plans, specifications, and other relevant documents for review. This may also include as-built documentation, which provides a detailed record of the actual construction process.

2. **Review:** The reviewers, who are typically experienced architects or construction professionals, carefully examine the submitted materials. They assess the design's feasibility, compliance with relevant codes and standards, and potential impact on the environment and community. They also evaluate the construction methods and materials for efficiency, safety, and sustainability.

3. **Feedback:** The reviewers provide feedback on their findings. This may include suggestions for revisions or improvements, questions for clarification, or recommendations for further research or testing. The feedback is usually provided in a written report, which is then returned to the submitter.

4. **Revision and Resubmission:** Based on the feedback, the architect or construction team may make revisions to the design or construction plans. The revised materials are then resubmitted for further review. This process may be repeated several times until the reviewers are satisfied with the quality and integrity of the work.

5. **Final Approval:** Once the reviewers have approved the design and construction plans, the project can proceed to the next stages of construction or implementation.

The peer review process can provide several benefits:

- **Quality Assurance:** Peer review helps to ensure that the design and construction meet high standards of quality and professionalism. It can identify potential issues or weaknesses that may not be apparent to the original team.

- **Risk Management:** By identifying potential issues early in the process, peer review can help to mitigate risks and prevent costly mistakes or delays later in the construction process.

- **Professional Development:** The feedback from peer review can provide valuable learning opportunities for architects and construction professionals, helping them to improve their skills and knowledge.

However, as with scholarly peer review, the construction peer review process is not without its challenges and criticisms. These may include concerns about the impartiality of reviewers, the potential for bias or conflict of interest, and the time and resources required for the review process. Despite these challenges, peer review remains a valuable tool for ensuring the quality and integrity of architectural construction.

#### 21.3b Instructor Feedback

Instructor feedback is another critical component of the construction review process. Unlike peer review, which is typically conducted by fellow architects or construction professionals, instructor feedback is provided by educators or mentors who have a deep understanding of architectural principles and construction techniques. This feedback can offer a unique perspective, as instructors often have a broader view of the field and can provide insights based on their extensive experience and knowledge.

The instructor feedback process in construction typically involves the following steps:

1. **Presentation:** The architect or construction team presents their design plans, specifications, and other relevant documents to the instructor. This presentation may be formal or informal, depending on the context, and it provides an opportunity for the instructor to gain a comprehensive understanding of the project.

2. **Discussion:** The instructor engages in a discussion with the architect or construction team, asking questions to clarify any unclear points and offering initial observations and suggestions. This dialogue can help to identify potential issues and areas for improvement that may not have been apparent during the design and construction process.

3. **Feedback:** The instructor provides detailed feedback on the project. This feedback may address a range of issues, from the conceptual design and architectural principles to the practical aspects of construction. The instructor may also provide guidance on how to address any identified issues and improve the project.

4. **Revision:** Based on the instructor's feedback, the architect or construction team revises their design or construction plans. This revision process is an opportunity for learning and growth, as it allows the architect or construction team to apply the instructor's feedback and improve their work.

5. **Follow-up:** After the revisions have been made, the instructor reviews the updated project and provides further feedback if necessary. This follow-up process ensures that the feedback has been effectively implemented and that the project meets the required standards of quality and integrity.

Instructor feedback, like peer review, is an essential part of the construction review process. It provides a valuable opportunity for architects and construction professionals to learn from experienced educators and mentors, enhancing their skills and knowledge and improving the quality of their work.

#### 21.3c Incorporating Feedback

Incorporating feedback is a crucial step in the construction review process. It involves taking the insights and suggestions provided by the instructor and applying them to the design or construction plans. This process is not merely about making changes to the project based on the feedback received; it is also about understanding the rationale behind the feedback and learning from it to improve future projects.

The process of incorporating feedback in construction typically involves the following steps:

1. **Understanding the Feedback:** The first step in incorporating feedback is to understand it thoroughly. This involves reviewing the feedback provided by the instructor, asking for clarification if needed, and understanding the reasons behind the suggestions or criticisms. It is essential to approach this step with an open mind and a willingness to learn.

2. **Planning the Changes:** Once the feedback is understood, the next step is to plan how to incorporate it into the project. This may involve revising the design plans, adjusting the construction techniques, or even rethinking the project's overall concept. The planning process should be systematic and detailed, outlining the specific changes to be made and the steps needed to implement them.

3. **Implementing the Changes:** After planning, the changes should be implemented in the project. This step requires careful execution to ensure that the changes are made accurately and effectively. It may also involve additional discussions with the instructor to ensure that the changes align with the feedback provided.

4. **Reviewing the Changes:** Once the changes have been implemented, they should be reviewed to ensure that they have effectively addressed the feedback. This review process may involve a re-presentation to the instructor, a self-review, or even a peer review. The goal is to ensure that the changes have improved the project and addressed the issues identified in the feedback.

5. **Reflecting on the Feedback:** The final step in incorporating feedback is reflection. This involves thinking about the feedback process, what was learned from it, and how it can be applied to future projects. Reflection is a critical part of learning and growth, and it can help architects and construction professionals to continually improve their work.

Incorporating feedback is a cyclical process that continues throughout the life of a project. It is a vital part of the construction review process, fostering learning, growth, and continual improvement. By effectively incorporating feedback, architects and construction professionals can enhance their projects, improve their skills, and contribute to the advancement of the field.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the intricate relationship between architectural construction and computation. We have delved into the principles that govern this relationship, the processes that facilitate it, and the applications that result from it. The chapter has highlighted the importance of computation in modern architectural construction, demonstrating how it has revolutionized the way architects design and construct buildings.

We have seen how computation allows architects to create more complex and innovative designs, while also improving the efficiency and accuracy of the construction process. It has also been shown how computation can aid in the analysis and optimization of architectural designs, leading to buildings that are not only aesthetically pleasing, but also structurally sound and environmentally friendly.

In conclusion, the integration of computation into architectural construction has opened up a world of possibilities, pushing the boundaries of what is achievable in architecture. As technology continues to advance, it is likely that computation will play an even greater role in architectural construction, leading to further innovations and advancements in the field.

## Chapter: Chapter 22: Student Proposal Presentations

### Introduction

The journey of architectural construction and computation is a complex one, filled with intricate processes, principles, and applications. As we delve into the 22nd chapter of this book, we shift our focus towards a critical aspect of this journey - Student Proposal Presentations. This chapter is designed to provide a comprehensive guide to students on how to effectively present their architectural proposals, a skill that is as important as the design process itself.

In the realm of architectural construction and computation, the ability to communicate one's ideas clearly and convincingly is paramount. This chapter will explore the various facets of proposal presentations, from the initial stages of preparation to the final delivery. It will provide insights into the art of storytelling in architecture, the importance of visual aids, and the role of computational tools in enhancing the presentation.

The chapter will also delve into the nuances of handling questions and feedback, a crucial part of any presentation. It will provide strategies for students to effectively respond to critiques and use them as a tool for refining their designs. Furthermore, the chapter will discuss the importance of self-evaluation and reflection in the process of proposal presentations.

In essence, this chapter aims to equip students with the necessary skills and knowledge to present their architectural proposals with confidence and clarity. It is a stepping stone towards becoming successful architects who can not only design innovative structures but also articulate their visions effectively. 

As we navigate through this chapter, remember that the art of presentation is a skill that can be honed with practice and perseverance. So, let's embark on this journey of learning and discovery together.

### Section: 22.1 Presenting and Defending Project Proposals

The process of presenting and defending project proposals is a critical skill in the field of architectural construction and computation. This section will guide you through the steps of preparing, presenting, and defending your project proposals effectively.

#### 22.1a Proposal Writing

The first step in the process of presenting and defending your project proposal is writing the proposal itself. A well-written proposal is clear, concise, and compelling. It should clearly articulate your project's objectives, methodology, and expected outcomes. 

When writing your proposal, consider the following:

1. **Objective**: Clearly state the purpose of your project. What problem are you trying to solve? What is the significance of your project in the context of architectural construction and computation?

2. **Methodology**: Describe the methods you will use to achieve your objectives. This could include computational tools, design processes, or construction techniques. Be sure to explain why you have chosen these methods and how they will help you achieve your objectives.

3. **Expected Outcomes**: What are the expected results of your project? How will these results contribute to the field of architectural construction and computation? 

4. **Timeline**: Provide a realistic timeline for your project. This should include key milestones and deliverables.

5. **Budget**: If applicable, provide a detailed budget for your project. This should include all costs associated with your project, including materials, labor, and computational resources.

Remember, your proposal is the first impression your audience will have of your project. Make sure it is well-structured, well-written, and compelling. Use visual aids where appropriate to enhance your proposal and make it more engaging.

In the next subsection, we will discuss how to prepare for the presentation of your project proposal.

#### 22.1b Presentation Skills

After writing a compelling proposal, the next step is to present it effectively. This subsection will guide you through the process of preparing and delivering a successful presentation.

1. **Preparation**: Preparation is key to a successful presentation. Familiarize yourself with your proposal and anticipate potential questions from your audience. Practice your presentation multiple times to ensure you can deliver it smoothly and confidently. Use visual aids such as slides, diagrams, or models to support your presentation and make it more engaging.

2. **Delivery**: During your presentation, speak clearly and at a moderate pace. Make eye contact with your audience to engage them and show confidence in your project. Use body language effectively to emphasize key points. Remember to pause occasionally to allow your audience to absorb the information.

3. **Handling Questions**: After your presentation, be prepared to answer questions from your audience. Listen carefully to each question, take a moment to formulate your response, and answer confidently. If you don't know the answer to a question, it's okay to admit it and offer to follow up later.

4. **Defending Your Proposal**: Defending your proposal is an opportunity to demonstrate your understanding of your project and your commitment to it. Be prepared to explain and justify your project's objectives, methodology, and expected outcomes. Show enthusiasm for your project and confidence in its success.

Remember, a successful presentation is not just about delivering information, but also about engaging your audience and convincing them of the value of your project. Practice, confidence, and enthusiasm are key to a successful presentation.

In the next subsection, we will discuss how to handle feedback and revise your project proposal based on the feedback received.

#### 22.1c Defense Strategies

Defending your project proposal is akin to a strategic battle. Just as in military tactics, you need to be prepared, have a clear plan, and be ready to adapt to changing circumstances. Here, we will discuss some defense strategies inspired by military tactics that can be applied to your project proposal defense.

1. **Bounded Defense**: This strategy is inspired by the bounded fire technique in squad assault. In this context, it means having a clear structure to your defense. You should have one element of your defense providing 'covering fire', i.e., addressing the main points of your proposal, while the second element maneuvers forward to provide 'covering fire' for the first, i.e., addressing potential criticisms and questions. This process is repeated until all aspects of your proposal have been defended. Just as in bounded fire, this strategy requires careful coordination and practice.

2. **Advance Under Cover**: This strategy is inspired by the technique of advancing under the cover of supporting units. In this context, it means using supporting evidence and arguments to bolster your main points. You should present your evidence in a way that suppresses potential criticisms, allowing your main points to advance unopposed. This strategy requires a deep understanding of your topic and a thorough review of the relevant literature.

3. **Consolidation**: This strategy is inspired by the interim phase of combat. After presenting your main points and supporting evidence, you should consolidate your position. This means summarizing your main points, addressing any remaining questions, and reinforcing the importance and relevance of your project. This strategy requires good communication skills and a clear understanding of your audience's concerns and interests.

4. **Defensive Positioning**: This strategy is inspired by the defensive phase of combat. In this context, it means being prepared for potential criticisms and having a plan to address them. You should anticipate potential criticisms and prepare responses in advance. This strategy requires a critical understanding of your project and the ability to think on your feet.

Remember, a successful defense is not just about responding to criticisms, but also about demonstrating your understanding of your project and your commitment to it. Just as in a military battle, preparation, strategy, and adaptability are key to a successful defense.

### Section: 22.2 Incorporating Feedback into Project Plans:

#### 22.2a Understanding Feedback

Feedback is a crucial part of any project, especially in the field of architectural construction and computation. It provides a way to measure the effectiveness of your project proposal and offers insights on how to improve it. Feedback can be categorized into two types: positive and negative.

Positive feedback is when the output signal is in phase with the input signal. This type of feedback is often seen as a validation of the current project plan and can be used to reinforce the strategies that are working well. However, it's important not to become complacent with positive feedback. Always look for ways to further improve and innovate.

Negative feedback, on the other hand, is when the output signal is out of phase by 180 with respect to the input signal. This type of feedback is often seen as criticism or a call for change. It's important to understand that negative feedback is not a sign of failure, but rather an opportunity for improvement. It provides valuable insights into the areas of your project that need adjustment or further development.

To illustrate, consider a cruise control system in a car. The car (controlled system) receives input from the combined torque of the engine and the changing slope of the road (disturbance). The car's speed (status) is measured by a speedometer. The error signal is the difference between the speed as measured by the speedometer and the target speed (set point). The controller interprets the speed to adjust the accelerator, commanding the fuel flow to the engine (the effector). The resulting change in engine torque, the feedback, combines with the torque exerted by the changing road grade to reduce the error in speed, minimizing the changing slope.

In the context of your project proposal, the feedback you receive can be seen as the error signal. It's the difference between your current project plan (status) and the ideal project plan (set point). Your task is to interpret this feedback and adjust your project plan accordingly, just like the controller in the cruise control system.

Remember, feedback is not a one-time event but a continuous process. It's important to regularly seek feedback and incorporate it into your project plan. This will not only improve the quality of your project but also increase your chances of success.

#### 22.2b Revision Strategies

Incorporating feedback into your project plan requires a systematic approach to revisions. This process can be broken down into three main steps: understanding the feedback, creating a revision plan, and implementing the changes.

##### Understanding the Feedback

As discussed in the previous section, feedback can be seen as an error signal, indicating the difference between your current project plan and the ideal. It's important to thoroughly understand this feedback before making any changes to your project plan. This involves analyzing the feedback to identify the specific areas of your project that need improvement. 

##### Creating a Revision Plan

Once you've identified the areas of your project that need improvement, the next step is to create a revision plan. This involves setting clear, measurable goals for each area of improvement and outlining the specific steps you'll take to achieve these goals. 

For example, if the feedback indicates that your project lacks a clear computational model, your revision plan might include steps like researching different computational models, selecting the most appropriate model for your project, and integrating this model into your project plan. 

##### Implementing the Changes

The final step in the revision process is to implement the changes outlined in your revision plan. This involves making the necessary adjustments to your project plan and continuously monitoring the effectiveness of these changes. 

Remember, the goal of revisions is not just to address the feedback you've received, but also to improve the overall quality of your project. Therefore, it's important to be open to further feedback and ready to make additional revisions as necessary.

In the context of architectural construction and computation, revisions can often involve complex calculations and computational models. For example, if you're revising the structural design of a building, you might need to use computational models to calculate the new load-bearing capacity of the structure. 

In such cases, it's important to ensure that your calculations are accurate and that your computational models are appropriate for the task at hand. This might involve consulting with experts in the field, reviewing relevant literature, or using specialized software tools.

Remember, the goal of revisions is not just to address the feedback you've received, but also to improve the overall quality of your project. Therefore, it's important to be open to further feedback and ready to make additional revisions as necessary. 

In conclusion, incorporating feedback into your project plan is a crucial part of the architectural construction and computation process. By understanding the feedback, creating a revision plan, and implementing the changes, you can improve the quality of your project and increase your chances of success.

#### 22.2c Continuous Improvement

Continuous improvement is a key aspect of incorporating feedback into project plans. It is a concept that is deeply rooted in Lean product development and other iterative methodologies, such as Agile software development. The idea is to continuously refine and improve your project plan based on the feedback you receive, with the ultimate goal of optimizing the quality and effectiveness of your project.

##### The Cycle of Continuous Improvement

Continuous improvement can be seen as a cycle that consists of four main steps: Plan, Do, Check, and Act (PDCA). This cycle, also known as the Deming Cycle, is a systematic series of steps for gaining valuable learning and knowledge for the continual improvement of a product or process.

1. **Plan**: Identify an opportunity and plan for change.
2. **Do**: Implement the change on a small scale.
3. **Check**: Use data to analyze the results of the change and determine whether it made a difference.
4. **Act**: If the change was successful, implement it on a wider scale and continuously assess your results. If the change did not work, begin the cycle again.

In the context of architectural construction and computation, this might involve planning a new computational model, implementing this model on a small scale (such as a single component of a building), checking the results through computational analysis, and then acting based on these results.

##### Incorporating Feedback into the Continuous Improvement Cycle

Feedback plays a crucial role in the continuous improvement cycle. It provides the data that you need to check the effectiveness of your changes and make informed decisions about how to proceed. 

For example, if you receive feedback that your computational model is not accurately predicting the structural behavior of a building, you can use this feedback to refine your model and improve its accuracy. This might involve adjusting the parameters of your model, incorporating new data, or even adopting a completely different model.

##### Continuous Improvement and CMMI

The Capability Maturity Model Integration (CMMI) is a process level improvement training and appraisal program. Administered by the CMMI Institute, it was developed at Carnegie Mellon University (CMU). It is required by many U.S. Government contracts, especially in software development. Turner & Jain (2002) argue that although there are large differences between CMMI and agile software development, both approaches have much in common. They suggest one should combine the different fragments of the methods into a new hybrid method. 

In the context of architectural construction and computation, the principles of continuous improvement can be applied to optimize the computational models and algorithms used in the design and construction process. By continuously refining these models based on feedback and performance data, architects and engineers can improve the accuracy and efficiency of their designs, ultimately leading to better buildings and structures. 

Remember, the goal of continuous improvement is not just to address the feedback you've received, but also to improve the overall quality of your project. Therefore, it's important to be open to further feedback and ready to make additional revisions as necessary.

### Section: 22.3 Refining Project Objectives and Scope:

#### 22.3a Defining Objectives

Defining objectives is a critical step in the process of refining the scope of a project. Objectives provide a clear direction and purpose for the project, and they serve as a guide for decision-making throughout the project lifecycle. They should be specific, measurable, achievable, relevant, and time-bound (SMART).

In the context of architectural construction and computation, objectives might include the development of a new computational model, the improvement of an existing model, or the application of a model to a specific architectural problem. These objectives should be aligned with the overall goals of the project, such as improving the efficiency of architectural design processes, enhancing the performance of buildings, or advancing the field of architectural computation.

##### Gordon Strong Automobile Objective

The Gordon Strong Automobile Objective provides a good example of how objectives can be defined in the context of architectural construction and computation. The objective of this project was to design a unique automobile showroom that would serve as a landmark and a tourist attraction. This objective was specific (design a showroom), measurable (the showroom should be unique and attract tourists), achievable (through architectural design and computation), relevant (to the client's business), and time-bound (the project had a defined timeline).

##### World Congress of Mountain Jews

The World Congress of Mountain Jews (WCMJ) also provides an example of defining objectives. The WCMJ's declared objective was to establish a single organization under which its interests could be declared to the world. This objective was specific (establish an organization), measurable (the organization should represent the interests of the WCMJ), achievable (through organizational and strategic planning), relevant (to the WCMJ's mission), and time-bound (the project had a defined timeline).

##### Goals Breakdown Structure

The Goals Breakdown Structure (GBS) is a useful tool for defining and organizing project objectives. The GBS follows two rules for decomposition: the first rule ensures the success of the layer above, and the second rule prevents the project from exhibiting extra scope and work that doesn't add value to the organization. This approach can help to ensure that project objectives are aligned with the overall goals of the project and that they contribute to the project's success.

In the context of architectural construction and computation, the GBS might include layers such as the project goal or mission statement, business objectives, and project requirements. Each of these layers would contain specific objectives that contribute to the overall goal of the project. For example, the business objectives layer might include objectives related to return on investment (ROI), market share, and efficiency improvement. The project requirements layer might include objectives related to the technical and functional requirements of the project.

In conclusion, defining objectives is a critical step in refining the project scope. By clearly defining and organizing objectives, project teams can ensure that their work is aligned with the overall goals of the project and that it contributes to the project's success.

#### 22.3b Scope Management

Scope management is an essential aspect of refining project objectives. It involves defining and controlling what is included and what is not included in a project. The scope of a project in architectural construction and computation can be quite broad, encompassing everything from the initial design concept to the final construction details. It can also include computational models, simulations, and other digital tools used in the design and construction process.

##### Scope Definition

The first step in scope management is to define the scope of the project. This involves clearly outlining the project's deliverables, requirements, and boundaries. For instance, in the Gordon Strong Automobile Objective, the scope included designing a unique automobile showroom that would serve as a landmark and a tourist attraction. In the World Congress of Mountain Jews project, the scope was to establish a single organization to represent its interests.

##### Scope Verification

Once the scope has been defined, it needs to be verified. This involves checking that the scope aligns with the project's objectives and that it is realistic and achievable. For example, in the case of the Gordon Strong Automobile Objective, the scope was verified by ensuring that the design of the showroom was feasible and that it could indeed serve as a unique landmark and tourist attraction.

##### Scope Control

The final step in scope management is scope control. This involves monitoring the project's progress and making adjustments as necessary to ensure that the project stays within its defined scope. In the case of the World Congress of Mountain Jews project, scope control might involve regularly checking that the organization is effectively representing the interests of the WCMJ and making adjustments as necessary.

In conclusion, refining project objectives and managing the project scope are crucial steps in the process of architectural construction and computation. They provide a clear direction for the project and ensure that it stays on track and achieves its intended outcomes.

#### 22.3c Project Planning

Project planning is a critical step in refining project objectives and scope. It involves outlining the tasks, resources, and timeframes required to complete the project. This process is crucial in architectural construction and computation, as it helps to ensure that the project is completed on time, within budget, and to the required quality standards.

##### Planning Process

The planning process begins with the development of a project plan. This plan outlines the project's objectives, deliverables, and scope, as well as the tasks, resources, and timeframes required to achieve these objectives. The project plan should be developed in consultation with all relevant stakeholders, including the project team, clients, and any external contractors or consultants.

The project plan should also include a detailed schedule, often referred to as the Working Schedule. This schedule combines a high-density, short-term look-ahead, similar in concept to that used in agile software development, with medium and long-term lower density schedule along the lines of that used in the waterfall model planning technique. This approach allows for flexibility and adaptability in the face of changing circumstances, while also ensuring that the project remains on track towards its long-term objectives.

##### Cost and Time Management

Effective project planning also involves careful cost and time management. The activities in the Working Schedule should be valued so that the schedule also functions as the pricing schedule. This allows for the prediction of current value for the purposes of interim payment and the ultimate out-turn cost for cost risk management purposes.

Time management is also crucial in project planning. The use of critical path network techniques combined with resource-based planning can help to ensure that the project is completed on time. Regular revisions of the schedule, based on the Rolling Wave planning principle, can also help to keep the project on track.

##### Progress Monitoring and Dispute Resolution

Monitoring progress is another important aspect of project planning. Progress should be recorded in a database identifying, at specified intervals, the resources used, productivity achieved, and earned value. This data can be used for subsequent progress updates of the schedule, as well as for benchmarking productivity achievable for quality assurance of the schedule and future planning.

Finally, project planning should also include provisions for dispute resolution. The emphasis should be on contemporaneous Issue Resolution by an appropriate expert. However, it is also important to have an escalating Dispute Resolution procedure in place, embracing negotiation, mediation, adjudication, and arbitration.

In conclusion, project planning is a critical step in refining project objectives and scope in architectural construction and computation. It involves careful planning, cost and time management, progress monitoring, and dispute resolution.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the critical role of student proposal presentations in the field of architectural construction and computation. We have seen how these presentations serve as a platform for students to showcase their understanding of the principles, processes, and applications of architectural construction and computation. They also provide an opportunity for students to demonstrate their ability to apply these concepts in a practical context, thereby bridging the gap between theory and practice.

The chapter has also highlighted the importance of effective communication in these presentations. As future architects, students need to be able to articulate their ideas clearly and convincingly, using both verbal and visual means. This not only helps them to convey their design concepts effectively, but also enables them to engage with their audience and solicit valuable feedback.

Finally, we have discussed the role of computation in architectural construction, and how it can be used to enhance the design process. Through the use of computational tools and techniques, students can explore new design possibilities, optimize their designs, and make more informed decisions.

In conclusion, student proposal presentations are a vital part of architectural education, providing students with a platform to apply their knowledge, demonstrate their skills, and engage with the architectural community. They also highlight the importance of effective communication and the role of computation in architectural construction.

### Exercises
