## Chapter: Chapter 23: Student Final Presentations

### Introduction

The journey through the world of architectural construction and computation is a complex and intricate one. It is a journey that requires a deep understanding of principles, a mastery of processes, and the ability to apply these in various contexts. This journey culminates in the final presentations, where students showcase their understanding and application of these principles and processes. 

Chapter 23, "Student Final Presentations", is a pivotal point in this book. It is here that we shift our focus from the theoretical to the practical, from the abstract to the concrete. This chapter is designed to guide students in preparing and delivering their final presentations, which serve as a testament to their learning journey.

The final presentations are not merely a demonstration of knowledge, but a synthesis of the principles, processes, and applications that have been discussed throughout the book. They are a platform for students to demonstrate their ability to integrate and apply these elements in a coherent and meaningful way. 

In this chapter, we will explore the key components of a successful final presentation, including the preparation process, the presentation itself, and the evaluation criteria. We will also discuss the importance of effective communication, both in terms of the presentation's content and its delivery.

While this chapter does not delve into specific section topics, it is a crucial part of the learning process. It is here that students have the opportunity to bring together all that they have learned, to demonstrate their understanding, and to showcase their ability to apply this knowledge in a practical context. 

In essence, Chapter 23, "Student Final Presentations", is the culmination of the journey through architectural construction and computation. It is a testament to the learning process, a demonstration of mastery, and a celebration of the knowledge and skills acquired.

### Section: 23.1 Presenting Completed Projects

#### 23.1a Presentation Preparation

The preparation for the final presentation is a critical phase that determines the success of the presentation. It involves a series of steps that are designed to ensure that the presentation is well-structured, coherent, and engaging. 

The first step in the preparation process is to review the project and identify the key points that need to be highlighted in the presentation. These key points should reflect the principles, processes, and applications that have been discussed throughout the course. They should also demonstrate the student's ability to integrate and apply these elements in a practical context.

Once the key points have been identified, the next step is to organize them in a logical and coherent manner. This involves creating an outline of the presentation, which serves as a roadmap for the presentation. The outline should include an introduction, a body, and a conclusion. The introduction should provide a brief overview of the project and its objectives. The body should elaborate on the key points, providing detailed explanations and examples. The conclusion should summarize the key points and highlight the implications of the project.

The third step in the preparation process is to develop the visual aids for the presentation. These can include diagrams, charts, graphs, and other visual elements that can help to illustrate the key points and make the presentation more engaging. The visual aids should be clear, concise, and relevant to the content of the presentation.

The final step in the preparation process is to rehearse the presentation. This involves practicing the delivery of the presentation, including the timing, the pacing, and the use of visual aids. Rehearsing the presentation can help to improve the fluency and confidence of the presenter, and it can also help to identify any potential issues that need to be addressed before the actual presentation.

In conclusion, the preparation for the final presentation is a critical phase that requires careful planning and preparation. It involves identifying the key points, organizing them in a logical and coherent manner, developing the visual aids, and rehearsing the presentation. By following these steps, students can ensure that their final presentations are well-structured, coherent, and engaging.

#### 23.1b Visual Aids

Visual aids play a crucial role in enhancing the effectiveness of a presentation. They provide a visual representation of the information being presented, making it easier for the audience to understand and remember the key points. In the context of architectural construction and computation, visual aids can include diagrams, charts, graphs, 3D models, animations, and even virtual reality simulations.

##### Diagrams and Charts

Diagrams and charts are effective tools for presenting complex data in a simplified and understandable manner. They can be used to illustrate the architectural design process, the computational algorithms used, the project timeline, and the project results, among other things. Tools such as Adobe Systems After Effects and Photoshop can be used to create high-quality diagrams and charts.

##### 3D Models and Animations

3D models and animations can provide a realistic and detailed representation of the architectural design. They can be used to showcase the design from different angles, to simulate the construction process, and to visualize the final result. Tools such as Autodesk Smoke, Flame, and Maya can be used to create 3D models and animations.

##### Virtual Reality Simulations

Virtual reality simulations can provide an immersive experience that allows the audience to explore the architectural design in a virtual environment. This can be particularly useful for presenting large-scale projects, such as VR warehouses. Tools such as Final Cut Pro and Avid can be used to create virtual reality simulations.

##### Use of Visual Aids

When using visual aids, it is important to ensure that they are clear, concise, and relevant to the content of the presentation. They should be used to support the key points, not to distract from them. It is also important to rehearse the use of visual aids to ensure that they are integrated smoothly into the presentation.

In conclusion, visual aids are a powerful tool for enhancing the effectiveness of a presentation. They can help to illustrate complex information, engage the audience, and leave a lasting impression. Therefore, it is essential to invest time and effort in developing high-quality visual aids for the final presentation.

#### 23.1c Public Speaking

Public speaking is an essential skill for presenting completed projects in architectural construction and computation. It involves the ability to communicate effectively to an audience, conveying complex ideas in a clear and engaging manner. This section will discuss the key aspects of public speaking, including preparation, delivery, and handling questions.

##### Preparation

Preparation is the first step in delivering a successful presentation. This involves understanding the audience, defining the purpose of the presentation, researching the topic, and organizing the content. 

Understanding the audience is crucial as it helps to tailor the presentation to their needs and interests. For instance, a presentation to a group of experts in architectural computation may require a different approach compared to a presentation to potential investors or clients.

Defining the purpose of the presentation helps to focus the content and structure. The purpose could be to inform, persuade, or inspire the audience.

Researching the topic ensures that the presenter is well-informed and able to answer questions confidently. It also helps to identify key points and supporting evidence.

Organizing the content involves creating a logical flow of ideas, starting with an introduction, followed by the main body, and ending with a conclusion. The introduction should grab the audience's attention and provide an overview of the presentation. The main body should present the key points in a clear and concise manner. The conclusion should summarize the key points and provide a call to action if necessary.

##### Delivery

Delivery is the next step in public speaking. This involves the use of voice, body language, and visual aids to communicate effectively. 

The voice should be clear and audible, with appropriate volume, pace, and intonation. It is important to vary the voice to maintain the audience's interest and to emphasize key points.

Body language, including facial expressions, gestures, and posture, can enhance the message and engage the audience. Eye contact is particularly important as it helps to establish a connection with the audience.

Visual aids, as discussed in the previous section, can enhance the presentation by providing a visual representation of the information. They should be used effectively to support the key points.

##### Handling Questions

Handling questions is the final step in public speaking. This involves listening carefully to the question, providing a clear and concise answer, and handling any follow-up questions or comments.

In conclusion, public speaking is a crucial skill for presenting completed projects in architectural construction and computation. It involves preparation, delivery, and handling questions, with the aim of communicating effectively and engaging the audience.

#### 23.2a Success Criteria

Evaluating the outcomes of architectural construction and computation projects requires a clear set of success criteria. These criteria should be defined at the outset of the project and should align with the project's objectives. 

##### Defining Success Criteria

Success criteria should be specific, measurable, achievable, relevant, and time-bound (SMART). They should be designed to stretch the project team while remaining achievable and relevant to the project's objectives. 

For instance, a success criterion for a construction project might be: "To complete the construction of the building within the allocated budget and timeline, meeting all the specified architectural and computational design requirements." This criterion is specific (complete the construction), measurable (within the budget and timeline), achievable (with the right resources and planning), relevant (to the project's objectives), and time-bound (within the project timeline).

##### Evaluating Success

The evaluation of project outcomes should be based on the defined success criteria. A project is considered successful if it meets or exceeds the defined criteria. 

Following the recommendation of Doerr, a target success rate of 70% can be set for key results. This encourages competitive goal-making that stretches the project team while keeping the risk low. If 100% of the key results are consistently being met, the success criteria should be reevaluated to ensure they are still challenging and relevant.

##### Leading and Lagging Indicators

When defining success criteria, it is important to consider both leading and lagging indicators. Leading indicators are measurable factors that can provide an early warning if the project is not on track. For example, in a construction project, a leading indicator might be the rate of progress against the project schedule.

On the other hand, lagging indicators are metrics that reflect the outcomes of the project. They are often harder to influence but provide a clear measure of the project's success. For example, the final cost of the project compared to the budget is a lagging indicator.

##### Best Practices

Following the best practices suggested by Ben Lamorte, success criteria should be limited to a small set reflecting the most important areas for measurable progress. They should focus on outcomes rather than outputs and should be distinguished from routine tasks and health metrics. 

In the context of architectural construction and computation, an outcome might be the successful integration of a computational design into a building, while an output might be the number of design iterations. Health metrics, such as team morale or client satisfaction, are important to monitor but should not be the primary focus for improvement. 

In conclusion, defining and evaluating success criteria is a critical part of project management in architectural construction and computation. It provides a clear framework for assessing project outcomes and for identifying areas for improvement.

#### 23.2b Outcome Evaluation

Outcome evaluation is a systematic process that assesses the extent to which a project has achieved its intended results. It involves the collection and analysis of data on project outputs, outcomes, and impacts. 

##### Outcome Measures

Outcome measures are tools used to assess the effectiveness of a project. They provide a quantitative means to determine if the project has met its objectives. In the context of architectural construction and computation, outcome measures could include the quality of the constructed building, the efficiency of the computational models used, or the satisfaction of the end-users.

One example of an outcome measure is the CORE-OM (Clinical Outcomes in Routine Evaluation-Outcome Measure). Although originally designed for mental health evaluations, the principles behind CORE-OM can be applied to architectural construction and computation projects. It covers domains of well-being, problems, functioning, and risk, which can be translated into domains relevant to construction and computation projects.

##### Outcome Evaluation Process

The outcome evaluation process typically involves the following steps:

1. **Identify Outcome Measures:** Determine what measures will be used to assess the project's success. These should align with the project's objectives and success criteria.

2. **Collect Data:** Gather data on the identified outcome measures. This could involve surveys, interviews, observations, or the analysis of project documents.

3. **Analyze Data:** Use statistical methods to analyze the collected data. This could involve comparing actual outcomes with expected outcomes, or comparing outcomes across different parts of the project.

4. **Interpret Results:** Draw conclusions from the data analysis. This could involve determining if the project met its objectives, identifying areas for improvement, or making recommendations for future projects.

5. **Report Findings:** Communicate the results of the outcome evaluation to relevant stakeholders. This could involve writing a report, giving a presentation, or discussing the findings in a meeting.

##### Outcome Evaluation in Practice

In practice, outcome evaluation can be a complex process. It requires careful planning, rigorous data collection and analysis, and thoughtful interpretation of results. However, when done well, it can provide valuable insights into a project's effectiveness and inform future project planning and implementation. 

For instance, the Public Health Agency of Canada uses Departmental Results Reports to evaluate the outcomes of its programs. These reports include both financial statements and outcome measures, providing a comprehensive view of program performance. Similarly, in architectural construction and computation projects, a combination of financial, technical, and user satisfaction measures could be used to evaluate project outcomes. 

In conclusion, outcome evaluation is a critical component of project management. It provides a means to assess the effectiveness of a project and inform future project planning and implementation. By carefully selecting outcome measures and rigorously collecting and analyzing data, project managers can gain valuable insights into their projects and make informed decisions about future projects.

#### 23.2c Lessons Learned

The final step in evaluating project outcomes is to reflect on the lessons learned throughout the project. This is a critical part of the evaluation process, as it allows for the identification of successful strategies and areas for improvement. It also provides an opportunity to apply these lessons to future projects, thereby improving the efficiency and effectiveness of architectural construction and computation.

##### Identifying Lessons Learned

The process of identifying lessons learned involves a thorough review of the project, including its objectives, processes, outcomes, and the outcome evaluation itself. This review should be conducted by the project team, and should involve input from all team members. The goal is to identify what worked well, what didn't, and why.

Some questions to consider during this review might include:

- Were the project's objectives met? If not, why not?
- Were there any unexpected outcomes? If so, what were they and why did they occur?
- Were there any challenges or obstacles encountered during the project? If so, how were they addressed?
- Were there any aspects of the project that were particularly successful? If so, what contributed to their success?
- Were there any aspects of the project that could be improved? If so, how?

##### Documenting Lessons Learned

Once the lessons learned have been identified, they should be documented in a formal Lessons Learned Report. This report should include a summary of the project, a description of the lessons learned, and recommendations for future projects. The report should be shared with all project stakeholders, and should be stored in a central location for future reference.

##### Applying Lessons Learned

The final step in the lessons learned process is to apply these lessons to future projects. This involves incorporating the lessons learned into the planning and execution of future projects, and continually reviewing and updating the Lessons Learned Report as new projects are completed.

In conclusion, the evaluation of project outcomes is a critical part of any architectural construction and computation project. It allows for the assessment of the project's success, the identification of areas for improvement, and the application of these lessons to future projects. By conducting a thorough outcome evaluation, project teams can continually improve their processes and outcomes, leading to more successful and efficient projects in the future.

#### 23.3a Self-Reflection

The process of self-reflection is an integral part of the design and construction process. It allows students to critically evaluate their own work, identify areas of strength and weakness, and develop strategies for improvement. Self-reflection also fosters a sense of self-awareness and self-efficacy, which are essential for successful architectural practice.

##### The Importance of Self-Reflection

Self-reflection is a powerful tool for personal and professional growth. It allows us to gain insights into our own thought processes, behaviors, and attitudes, and to use these insights to improve our performance and effectiveness. In the context of architectural construction and computation, self-reflection can help us to better understand our design decisions, to evaluate the effectiveness of our computational strategies, and to identify areas where we can improve.

##### Self-Reflection Exercises

There are several exercises that can facilitate self-reflection. These exercises are designed to foster self-kindness, mindfulness, and feelings of common humanity, which are key components of self-compassion. They have been shown to be effective in increasing self-compassion, along with increases in self-efficacy, optimism, and mindfulness. 

###### How would you treat a friend?

This exercise asks you to imagine that you are comforting a close friend who is going through a tough situation, such as a challenging design problem or a construction setback. You are then asked to compare and contrast how you react internally to your own struggles, and to endeavor applying the same loving kindness to yourself that you would apply to a friend.

###### Self-compassion break

This exercise is to be used during times of acute distress, such as when a design is not working out as planned or a construction problem seems insurmountable. You are asked to focus on the stressful event or situation, and then to repeat several prompts to yourself, each of which emphasizes one of the three main tenets of self-compassion: mindfulness, common humanity, and self-kindness.

###### Exploring through writing

In this exercise, you are asked to focus on an aspect of your work that you believe to be an imperfection, and that makes you feel inadequate. Once you have brought this issue to mind, you are asked to write a letter to yourself from the perspective of an unconditionally loving imaginary friend. This exercise can help you to generate feelings of compassion for yourself, and to see your perceived imperfections in a new light.

##### Applying Self-Reflection to Architectural Practice

The process of self-reflection can be applied to all aspects of architectural practice, from the initial design phase to the final construction phase. By regularly engaging in self-reflection, you can develop a deeper understanding of your own design and construction processes, and can continually improve your skills and effectiveness. 

In conclusion, self-reflection is a powerful tool for personal and professional growth in the field of architectural construction and computation. By fostering self-compassion, mindfulness, and a sense of common humanity, self-reflection can help you to navigate the challenges of architectural practice, and to continually improve your skills and effectiveness.

#### 23.3b Peer Feedback

Peer feedback is another crucial aspect of the design and construction process. It provides an opportunity for students to learn from each other, to gain new perspectives on their work, and to improve their communication and collaboration skills. Peer feedback can be particularly valuable in the context of architectural construction and computation, where the complexity of the tasks often requires a diverse range of skills and perspectives.

##### The Importance of Peer Feedback

Peer feedback can provide insights that might be overlooked in self-reflection. It allows students to see their work through the eyes of others, to understand how their design decisions and computational strategies are perceived by their peers, and to learn from the experiences and expertise of others. Peer feedback can also foster a sense of community and collaboration, as students work together to solve problems and improve their designs.

##### Peer Feedback Exercises

There are several exercises that can facilitate peer feedback. These exercises are designed to promote constructive criticism, empathy, and mutual respect, which are key components of effective feedback.

###### Design Critique

In this exercise, students present their designs to a small group of peers, who then provide feedback on the design. The feedback should be specific, constructive, and focused on the design rather than the designer. The presenter should listen to the feedback without defending or justifying their design decisions, and should then reflect on the feedback and consider how it can be used to improve the design.

###### Code Review

In this exercise, students share their computational strategies and code with a peer, who then reviews the code for clarity, efficiency, and correctness. The reviewer should provide specific, constructive feedback, and the coder should consider the feedback and make any necessary revisions to the code.

###### Group Reflection

In this exercise, a group of students reflect together on a shared experience, such as a design project or a construction task. Each student shares their own perspective on the experience, and the group then discusses the different perspectives and reflects on what they can learn from each other.

These exercises can be powerful tools for learning and growth, but they require a supportive and respectful environment. It is important to establish clear guidelines for feedback, to promote a culture of mutual respect and empathy, and to provide training and support for students in giving and receiving feedback.

#### 23.3c Continuous Learning

Continuous learning is an essential part of the architectural construction and computation process. It involves the ongoing acquisition and application of knowledge, skills, and attitudes that enable students to improve their design and computational abilities over time. Continuous learning is not only about learning from one's own experiences but also about learning from the experiences of others, from the feedback received, and from the ongoing developments in the field of architectural construction and computation.

##### The Importance of Continuous Learning

In the rapidly evolving field of architectural construction and computation, continuous learning is crucial for staying up-to-date with the latest technologies, methodologies, and best practices. It allows students to adapt to new challenges, to improve their problem-solving abilities, and to enhance their creativity and innovation.

Continuous learning also fosters a growth mindset, which is the belief that one's abilities can be developed through dedication and hard work. This mindset encourages students to embrace challenges, to persist in the face of setbacks, to see effort as the path to mastery, and to learn from criticism.

##### Continuous Learning Exercises

There are several exercises that can facilitate continuous learning. These exercises are designed to promote self-reflection, peer feedback, and lifelong learning.

###### Self-Reflection

In this exercise, students reflect on their design and construction process, considering what went well, what could have been done differently, and what they have learned. This reflection should be documented in a learning journal, which can be reviewed periodically to track progress and identify areas for improvement.

###### Peer Learning

In this exercise, students learn from each other by sharing their experiences, insights, and lessons learned. This can be done through group discussions, presentations, or collaborative projects. Peer learning not only enhances knowledge and skills but also fosters teamwork, communication, and empathy.

###### Lifelong Learning

In this exercise, students develop a personal learning plan, which outlines their learning goals, strategies, and resources. This plan should be updated regularly to reflect new interests, challenges, and opportunities. Lifelong learning involves not only formal education but also informal learning activities, such as reading, attending seminars, participating in online courses, and engaging in professional networks.

In conclusion, continuous learning is a vital part of the architectural construction and computation process. It empowers students to improve their abilities, to adapt to new challenges, and to contribute to the advancement of the field. As the famous quote by Alvin Toffler goes, "The illiterate of the 21st century will not be those who cannot read and write, but those who cannot learn, unlearn, and relearn."
